($\Psi \approx 0.27$), meaning the residual flow concentrates
into a single plane --- the repetition plane.}
\label{fig:bca-lag}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
\textbf{BCA detects the 9-token repetition cycle as a characteristic
signature in directed flow}
(observed in Qwen2.5-7B across three prompt categories).

\emph{Statistical caveat:} all values in this section are point
estimates from single generation runs of 50 decode steps.  The
effective sample size for lag-$\tau$ estimates is $S - \tau$, which
at $\tau = 9$ is only 41~steps.  The patterns described below are
indicative and require multi-run replication to establish confidence
intervals (Section~\ref{sec:pred-open}).

\medskip
At lag $\tau = 9$ (the repetition cycle length), the Adversarial
prompt shows a simultaneous \emph{drop} in total directed flow and
\emph{spike} in coherence.  The mechanism is straightforward:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item When $\tau$ matches the cycle length, the lagged frontier
  $\tilde{F}_{s+\tau}$ is approximately aligned with
  $\tilde{F}_s$ --- the trajectory has nearly returned to where it
  started.
\item Gross displacement across one full cycle is therefore small,
  so total directed flow $\Omega$ drops.
\item The small residual that does \emph{not} cancel is dominated
  by the single plane of the orbit, so coherence $\Psi$ rises.
\end{itemize}
This simultaneous drop-and-spike is the BCA signature of a
repeating cycle.

By contrast, Retrieval's asymmetry ratio grows monotonically from
$0.23$ at lag~1 to $0.54$ at lag~15: the generation has a preferred
forward direction that strengthens at longer horizons --- a
pattern that PCA cannot detect.  Reasoning remains the least
directional at all lags, consistent with its irregular path.
\end{finding}

\subsection*{Rolling BCA: how directed flow evolves}

Computing BCA in a rolling window tracks how the directional
structure changes during generation
(Figure~\ref{fig:bca-rolling}).  Each window consists of 15
consecutive decode steps with lag $\tau = 1$.  Windows overlap by
14~steps (sliding by one step at a time).  Centering and
lag-moment computation are performed independently within each
window; no global centering is applied.  The curves are plotted
raw (unsmoothed), with each point aligned to the centre step of
its window.

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/bca_rolling_frontier.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Rolling BCA} (window 15, lag 1).
(a)~Directed flow: Adversarial starts high and decays as the
repetition settles in; Retrieval increases as generation builds
directional structure.
(b)~Coherence: Adversarial is consistently the highest
(${\sim}0.20$--$0.23$), confirming single-plane organisation
throughout.
(c)~Asymmetry ratio: Retrieval increases steadily (growing
directional structure); Adversarial decreases as the repetition
becomes more regular.}
\label{fig:bca-rolling}
\end{figure}

\subsection*{BCA phase portrait: directions of flow vs.\ directions
  of spread}

Projecting the frontier trajectories onto the principal planes of
$B_\tau$ (instead of the principal components of the covariance)
shows where the \emph{sequential structure} lives
(Figure~\ref{fig:bca-phase}).

Given the $j$-th principal plane spanned by orthonormal columns
$q_{2j-1}, q_{2j}$ from the decomposition~\eqref{eq:bca-schur},
the projection of each frontier descriptor is:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:bca-projection}
  z_s^{(j)} = \bigl(q_{2j-1}^\top \tilde{F}_s, \;\;
    q_{2j}^\top \tilde{F}_s\bigr) \;\in\; \mathbb{R}^2.
\end{equation}
This is the BCA analogue of the PCA projection in
equation~\eqref{eq:pca-projection}: instead of projecting onto
directions of maximum variance, we project onto planes of maximum
directed flow.

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/bca_phase_portrait.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{BCA phase portrait.}
Each trajectory is projected onto the top two principal planes of
directed flow ($\omega_1 = 2.12$, $\omega_2 = 1.40$) using
equation~\eqref{eq:bca-projection}.
Arrows show the temporal direction.
Adversarial (purple) traces a large, structured orbit ---
its directed flow dominates both planes.
Retrieval (blue) and Reasoning (orange) stay compact near the
origin.  Compare with Figure~\ref{fig:phase-portrait}: PCA shows
\emph{where} each trajectory spreads; BCA shows \emph{which
direction} it flows.}
\label{fig:bca-phase}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
\textbf{BCA separates three generation regimes by their directional
structure}
(observed in Qwen2.5-7B across three prompt categories).

\medskip
\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{} l c c c l @{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Prompt} & \textbf{Dir.\ flow} $\Omega$
  & \textbf{Coherence} $\Psi$ & $\|B\|/\|C\|$
  & \textbf{Description} \\
\midrule
Retrieval   & 32.2 & 0.08 & 0.23 & Steady forward drift \\
Reasoning   & 24.8 & 0.08 & 0.16 & Weak, dispersed movement \\
Adversarial & 34.4 & 0.18 & 0.21 & Concentrated single-plane cycle \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\smallskip
\noindent\emph{Note:} These are single-run point estimates (50 decode
steps); see the statistical caveat above.

\medskip
PCA and BCA are complementary:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{PCA} shows that the Adversarial trajectory covers the
  most territory (highest variance).
\item \textbf{BCA} shows that it is also the most \emph{organised}
  (highest coherence), with its sequential structure concentrated
  in one plane --- the repetition plane.
\item Retrieval has the highest asymmetry ratio at longer lags:
  its trajectory has the strongest forward direction, even
  though PCA shows less variance than Adversarial.
\item Reasoning scores lowest on all BCA metrics, consistent with
  its irregular, multi-directional path.
\end{itemize}

The decomposition $M_\tau = C_\tau + B_\tau$ (symmetric $+$
antisymmetric) is conceptually analogous to the versor decomposition
$T = RP$ from Chapter~\ref{chap:versor} (stretching $+$ rotation):
both separate an object into a symmetric and an antisymmetric part.
The analogy should not be pushed too far --- $T = RP$ is
multiplicative and acts on single-layer transformations, while
$M_\tau = C_\tau + B_\tau$ is additive and characterises a time
series --- but both share the principle that the antisymmetric
component carries the oriented, directional information.
\end{finding}

\begin{gabox}
\textbf{Generation as sequential extension of the layer--token
grid.}
Parts~I--IV analysed the hidden-state grid $H(l, t)$ for a fixed
token sequence.  During generation, the model extends this grid one
column at a time.  Each new column is determined by:
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item The existing hidden states (cached in the KV store).
\item The model's forward pass (the transition map~$\Phi$).
\item The frontier column at the previous step.
\end{enumerate}

The static analysis of Parts~I--IV supplies the \emph{starting
point}; the frontier analysis of Part~V describes how the grid
\emph{grows}.  PCA captures the spread of this growth; BCA
captures its direction.
\end{gabox}


% ── 15.7 ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\section{Open questions and limitations}
\label{sec:pred-open}

Several directions remain open:

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Sliding-window metric.}
  For generations longer than $\sim\!100$ tokens, the fixed prompt
  metric may become inadequate
  (Section~\ref{sec:frontier-gauge}).  A sliding-window approach
  that re-estimates the whitening metric periodically could extend
  the framework to unbounded generation.

\item \textbf{Threshold calibration.}
  The periodicity threshold $\tau_{\mathrm{rep}} = 0.4$ was
  validated on three prompts from one model
  (Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier-capacity}).  Systematic calibration
  across model families (Qwen, LLaMA, Mistral) and generation
  strategies (beam search, nucleus sampling) is needed.

\item \textbf{Targeted steering.}
  Section~\ref{sec:pred-steering} showed that random-plane
  perturbations cannot break the repetition orbit.  The next step
  is to steer in the \emph{orbit's own plane} --- identified from
  the periodic Cayley bivectors --- and test whether this
  escapes the attractor.

\item \textbf{Approximate repetition.}
  The geometric detector can in principle detect \emph{approximate}
  repetition (periodic geometry without identical tokens), which
  string matching cannot.  Constructing test cases for this
  scenario is important for validating the detector's advantage.

\item \textbf{Multi-model comparison.}
  How does frontier capacity scale with model size?  Does
  Qwen2.5-32B use more capacity per step, or the same capacity
  with higher effective rank?  The scaling experiment from
  Chapter~\ref{chap:capacity} should be extended to the frontier.

\item \textbf{Conformal and projective GA.}
  Chapter~\ref{chap:conclusion} noted that projective GA
  $\mathrm{Cl}(k,0,1)$ can represent the residual connection's
  additive structure as a translation.  At the frontier, this
  would unify the rotation (grade-2) and residual
  (grade-1/translation) components into a single algebraic object.

\item \textbf{Statistical reliability of BCA diagnostics.}
  The BCA metrics are estimated from short trajectories
  ($S \approx 40$--$60$ decode steps), yielding effective sample
  sizes as low as $S - \tau \approx 31$ at lag~$\tau = 9$.
  Bootstrap confidence intervals over multiple generation runs,
  or permutation tests that shuffle the temporal order, would
  quantify the reliability of the regime separations in
  Section~\ref{sec:pred-bca} and establish whether the observed
  differences (e.g.\ coherence $0.18$ vs.\ $0.08$) are
  statistically significant.
\end{enumerate}


% ── 15.8 ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\section{Summary: the three levels of frontier analysis}
\label{sec:pred-summary}

Part~V has developed three complementary levels of frontier
analysis:

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch17_part_v_summary.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Part V summary: descriptive $\to$ diagnostic
$\to$ predictive.}
\textbf{(a)}~Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier}: frontier rotation
angle (what happens).
\textbf{(b)}~Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier-capacity}: frontier
capacity (why it happens).
\textbf{(c)}~Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier-prediction}: online
periodicity with detection (when to act).
The three prompts separate cleanly at each level.}
\label{fig:part-v-summary}
\end{figure}

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.4}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}l L{4.5cm} L{4.5cm}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Level} & \textbf{Chapter} & \textbf{Key output} \\
\midrule
Descriptive &
  Ch.~\ref{chap:frontier}: The Decode Frontier &
  Angles, bivectors, commutators, drift, holonomy \\
Diagnostic &
  Ch.~\ref{chap:frontier-capacity}: Capacity and Quality &
  $C_{\mathrm{acc}}(s)$, $\nu(s)$, grade ratio,
  quality scores \\
Predictive &
  Ch.~\ref{chap:frontier-prediction}: Prediction and
  Intervention &
  Online monitoring, repetition detection, steering
  targets, phase portrait \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{keyidea}
\textbf{The model's geometry is not a metaphor.}

Throughout this book, we have used Geometric Algebra not as an
analogy but as a \emph{computational framework}: bivectors
name the planes where the model rotates representations; rotors
compose those rotations; commutators measure their interaction;
holonomy detects curvature; capacity quantifies complexity.

At the frontier, these quantities become a \textbf{real-time
diagnostic}: they can be computed during generation, monitored for
anomalies, and used to identify intervention targets.  The
geometry is the computation --- and the computation can be
observed, diagnosed, and steered through its geometry.
\end{keyidea}

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={Online monitoring and detection pipeline}]
from layer_time_ga import (
    generate_with_frontier,
    whiten_frontier,
    compute_frontier_ga,
    frontier_capacity,
    online_quality_scores,
    detect_repetition_online,
    frontier_steering_target,
)

# ... (generate, whiten, compute GA, capacity as before) ...

# Online quality scores
online = online_quality_scores(ga, cap, threshold=0.4)
print(f"Detection step: {online.detection_step}")

# Formal repetition detection
det = detect_repetition_online(cap, threshold=0.4)
if det.detected:
    print(f"Repetition at step {det.detection_step}, "
          f"period {det.detected_period}")

# Identify steering target at detected step
if det.detected:
    target = frontier_steering_target(ga, cap, det.detection_step)
    print(f"Steer at layer {target['layer']}, "
          f"angle {target['angle']:.3f}")
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{tryit}
\textbf{Notebook: Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier-prediction}.}
The companion notebook
\texttt{ch17\_frontier\_prediction.ipynb} demonstrates the full
prediction and intervention pipeline.

\medskip
\textbf{Exercises.}

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item Lower the detection threshold to $\tau = 0.3$ and rerun.
  Does Reasoning now trigger a false positive?  What is the
  minimum threshold that avoids false positives on your three
  prompts?

\item Implement \emph{targeted} steering: at the detection step,
  extract the Cayley bivector's principal plane vectors (in the
  whitened space), map them back to the raw hidden-state space
  using the inverse whitening transform, and use them as the
  perturbation plane.  Does this break the Adversarial orbit?

\item Compute the BCcos between step~$s$ and step~$s - P$
  (one period back) for the Adversarial prompt.  A BCcos near
  $+1$ at lag~$P = 9$ confirms that the frontier traces the same
  oriented plane each cycle.

\item Extend the phase portrait to 200 generation steps.
  Does the Retrieval prompt's trajectory remain smooth, or does
  it eventually enter a more complex region of phase space?
\end{enumerate}
\end{tryit}


% ╔════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╗
\part{Synthesis}
\label{part:synthesis}
% ╚════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╝


% ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\chapter{Diagnosing and Steering}
\label{chap:applications}
% ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

\begin{whycare}
This chapter applies everything you have learned to four practical
problems: interpreting what transformers compute, detecting when a model
ignores context, diagnosing hallucinations, and steering model behaviour
via plane-specific interventions.  Each application uses the full GA
toolkit --- bivectors (Chapter~\ref{chap:bivectors}), holonomy
(Chapter~\ref{chap:holonomy}), commutators
(Chapter~\ref{chap:commutators}), dependency
(Chapter~\ref{chap:dependency}) --- and introduces new mathematical
objects (the Cayley bivector from Chapter~\ref{chap:rotors} and the
directional--radial decomposition) that connect GA to modern
interpretability research.
\end{whycare}

% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\section{Application 1: Mechanistic interpretability through GA}
\label{sec:mech-interp}
% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Mechanistic interpretability aims to understand \emph{what}
transformers compute and \emph{how} they compute it --- not merely
which inputs produce which outputs, but what internal structures
mediate the computation.  The dominant approach uses sparse
autoencoders to decompose activations into monosemantic features.
This section develops an alternative and complementary lens:
\textbf{geometric algebra provides mechanistic interpretability at the
layer level}, decomposing each layer transition into rotation
(semantic redirection), metric deformation (energy reallocation),
and curvature (cross-layer interaction).

\subsection{The interpretability gap}

Standard interpretability methods operate at two extremes:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Token-level} methods (attention maps, gradient
  attribution, probing classifiers) identify \emph{which} tokens or
  features matter but not how they are transformed across layers.
\item \textbf{Global} methods (circuit analysis, sparse dictionary
  learning) identify computational subgraphs but require
  task-specific supervision or expensive dictionary training.
\end{itemize}
Neither provides a \emph{layer-by-layer mechanistic account} of how
the representation changes from input embedding to output logit.
The versor decomposition fills this gap.

\subsection{The versor decomposition as a mechanistic lens}

Recall from Chapter~\ref{chap:versor} that each layer transition
decomposes as
\begin{equation}
  T^{(l)} = \rotor{l} \cdot \metric{l},
\end{equation}
where $\rotor{l}$ is the rotor (orthogonal factor) and $\metric{l}$ is
the metric deformation (symmetric positive-definite factor).  Each
factor answers a different mechanistic question:

\begin{gabox}
\textbf{Two mechanistic components of a layer transition.}
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Rotor $\rotor{l}$ (semantic redirection):} Which
  semantic directions are rotated into which others?  The bivector
  generator $\bivec{l} = \log \rotor{l}$ identifies the \emph{plane} of
  rotation --- the pair of semantic dimensions being mixed --- and the
  rotation angle $\theta^{(l)} = \|\bivec{l}\|_F$ measures the magnitude
  of the transformation.
\item \textbf{Metric $\metric{l}$ (energy reallocation):}  Which
  features are amplified and which are suppressed?  The eigenvalues of
  $\metric{l}$ give the stretch spectrum: $\sigma_i > 1$ means
  amplification along eigenvector $e_i$; $\sigma_i < 1$ means
  suppression.
\end{enumerate}
\end{gabox}

\noindent
This decomposition is \emph{model-agnostic} (it applies to any
transformer), \emph{unsupervised} (no labelled data or task-specific
probes), and \emph{complete} (every layer transition is decomposed,
not just those selected by a probing objective).

\subsection{What each GA quantity reveals}

Table~\ref{tab:ga-interp} maps the GA quantities developed in
Chapters~\ref{chap:rotors}--\ref{chap:dependency} to their
interpretability counterparts.

\begin{table}[ht]
\centering\small
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.4}
\begin{tabular}{@{}l l l@{}}
\toprule
\rowcolor{Navy!15}
\textbf{GA quantity} & \textbf{Mechanistic meaning} &
  \textbf{Chapter} \\
\midrule
Rotation angle $\theta^{(l)}$ &
  How much semantic content changes at layer $l$ &
  \ref{chap:rotors} \\
Bivector $\bivec{l}$ &
  \emph{Which} semantic plane is rotated &
  \ref{chap:planes} \\
Plane similarity &
  Do adjacent layers rotate the same features? &
  \ref{chap:planes} \\
Condition number $\kappa^{(l)}$ &
  How selectively the layer amplifies features &
  \ref{chap:frames} \\
Effective rank &
  Dimensionality of the active subspace &
  \ref{chap:frames} \\
Commutator $[\bivec{l}, \bivec{l+1}]$ &
  Non-commutativity: can layers be reordered? &
  \ref{chap:commutators} \\
Holonomy $\Omega$ &
  Curvature: does parallel transport depend on path? &
  \ref{chap:holonomy} \\
Capacity $C_{\mathrm{acc}}$ &
  Total non-commutative complexity &
  \ref{chap:capacity} \\
Dependency $D_l$ &
  Which layers matter for the output? &
  \ref{chap:dependency} \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\caption{GA quantities and their mechanistic interpretations.}
\label{tab:ga-interp}
\end{table}

\subsection{Three mechanistic insights from GA}

We illustrate each insight using the three standard prompts on
Qwen2.5-7B ($k = 256$): Retrieval (19~tokens), Reasoning
(29~tokens), and Adversarial (18~tokens).
Figure~\ref{fig:mech-interp} shows the composite GA mechanistic
profile.

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_mech_interp_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{GA mechanistic profile for three prompt types (Qwen2.5-7B,
  $k = 256$).  Green/blue/orange shading marks the early, middle,
  and late phases.
  (a)~Rotation angle: Reasoning rotates more overall;
  all three show the three-phase structure.
  (b)~Commutator norm: the peak layer shifts with prompt type,
  indicating different loci of cross-layer interaction.
  (c)~Effective work $D_l \times \theta^{(l)}$
  (Chapter~\ref{chap:dependency}): the mechanistic bottleneck is
  concentrated in the first few layers for Retrieval but spreads
  deeper for Reasoning.}
\label{fig:mech-interp}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{Insight 1: The three-phase structure reveals functional
stages.}
The rotor angle profile (panel~(a)) consistently shows three phases
across all prompts and models studied:
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Early layers} ($l \lesssim L/4$): large rotation
  angles, diverse planes --- the model is building its representation
  by mixing many semantic dimensions.
\item \textbf{Middle layers} ($L/4 \lesssim l \lesssim 3L/4$):
  moderate angles with high plane similarity --- the model refines
  its representation within a stable set of semantic planes.
\item \textbf{Late layers} ($l \gtrsim 3L/4$): declining angles,
  increasing condition numbers --- the model is compressing
  information into the output-relevant subspace.
\end{enumerate}
This is a \emph{mechanistic} decomposition of the forward pass: each
phase has a distinct geometric signature that is visible without
any task-specific supervision.

Figure~\ref{fig:mech-phases} and Table~\ref{tab:mech-phases}
quantify the per-phase statistics.

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_mech_interp_phases_3prompts.pdf}
\caption{Per-phase comparison of rotation angle and metric selectivity
  across the three prompt types (Qwen2.5-7B).  Reasoning shows
  uniformly higher rotation angles; Adversarial shows the lowest
  early-phase angles.}
\label{fig:mech-phases}
\end{figure}

\begin{table}[ht]
\centering\small
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}
\begin{tabular}{@{}l C{1.5cm} C{1.5cm} C{1.5cm} C{1.5cm} C{1.5cm} C{2.2cm}@{}}
\toprule
\rowcolor{Navy!15}
\textbf{Prompt} &
  $\bar\theta_{\text{early}}$ &
  $\bar\theta_{\text{mid}}$ &
  $\bar\theta_{\text{late}}$ &
  $C_{\mathrm{acc}}$ &
  Peak $[B,B']$ &
  Top-3 eff.\ work \\
\midrule
Retrieval   & 7.54 & 8.29 & 7.86 & 585 & L1  & 0, 1, 2 \\
Reasoning   & 9.83 & 9.39 & 10.43 & 659 & L4  & 0, 1, 4 \\
Adversarial & 7.31 & 8.24 & 7.68 & 613 & L3  & 0, 3, 4 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\caption{GA mechanistic fingerprint for three prompt types
  (Qwen2.5-7B, $k = 256$).  $\bar\theta$: mean rotation angle
  (rad) in each phase.  $C_{\mathrm{acc}}$: accumulated
  non-commutativity.  Peak~$[B,B']$: layer transition with the
  largest commutator norm.  Top-3 eff.\ work: the three layer
  transitions with the highest $D_l \times \theta^{(l)}$.}
\label{tab:mech-phases}
\end{table}

\begin{finding}
\textbf{Prompt-dependent mechanistic fingerprint} (Qwen2.5-7B,
3~prompts).
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Reasoning} has the highest rotation angles in all
  three phases ($\bar\theta_{\text{late}} = 10.43$ vs.\
  $7.86$/$7.68$) and the highest accumulated capacity
  ($C_{\mathrm{acc}} = 659$).  More computation is needed: the model
  rotates more aggressively and the rotation planes are less
  commutative.
\item \textbf{Adversarial} shows the lowest early-phase rotation
  ($\bar\theta_{\text{early}} = 7.31$) but a peak commutator at
  layer~3, suggesting the model's refusal mechanism engages in the
  first few layers before the representation stabilises.
\item \textbf{Retrieval} concentrates effective work in the first
  three layers (0, 1, 2), consistent with early factual look-up
  followed by relatively passive propagation through deeper layers.
\end{itemize}
\end{finding}

\paragraph{Insight 2: Commutators identify non-trivially interacting
layers.}
When the commutator $\|[\bivec{l}, \bivec{l+1}]\|_F$ is large
(panel~(b)), the two layers perform rotations in genuinely different
planes --- their effects cannot be disentangled by reordering.
This is a geometric signature of \emph{cross-layer information
mixing}: the computation at layer $l+1$ depends on the specific
representation produced by layer $l$, not just its magnitude.

The peak commutator layer shifts with prompt type: L1 for Retrieval,
L4 for Reasoning, L3 for Adversarial.  This shift is mechanistically
informative --- it tells us \emph{where} in the network the
computation requires non-commutative sequential processing, and that
location depends on the semantic nature of the input.

Conversely, small commutators indicate that adjacent layers rotate in
nearly the same plane and could, in principle, be composed into a
single larger rotation.  This identifies \emph{redundant depth} ---
layers that could be pruned or merged without significant loss of
computational power.

\paragraph{Insight 3: Dependency-weighted bivectors locate the
mechanistic bottleneck.}
The effective work $D_l \times \theta^{(l)}$ (panel~(c),
Chapter~\ref{chap:dependency}) identifies layers where large
rotations coincide with high gradient-based dependency.  These are
the \emph{mechanistic bottlenecks}: layers where the output is most
sensitive to the specific rotation being performed.  Intervening at
these layers (amplifying, suppressing, or redirecting the rotation)
has the greatest downstream effect.

The top-3 effective work layers are \{0, 1, 2\} for Retrieval
(early concentration), \{0, 1, 4\} for Reasoning (extending deeper),
and \{0, 3, 4\} for Adversarial (skipping the initial layers).
This matches the intuition that retrieval is a shallow look-up,
reasoning requires deeper sequential processing, and adversarial
prompts trigger an early intervention mechanism.

\subsection{Orthogonality blindness: when geometry explains failure}

A striking mechanistic phenomenon illustrates the power of the
geometric lens.  Consider a model used as an NLI (natural language
inference) classifier.  Some models write perfectly reasoned
explanations (``These statements contradict each other because...'')
yet assign highest probability to the \emph{wrong} label.

The geometric diagnosis is direct:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item Extract class-mean hidden states $\mu_c$ and output-weight
  rows $w_c$ for each class.
\item Compute the \textbf{Fisher discriminant ratio} along each
  decision boundary $d_{ij} = w_i - w_j$:
  \begin{equation}
    F_{ij} = \frac{(\mu_i - \mu_j)^2}{\sigma_i^2 + \sigma_j^2}
    \bigg|_{\text{projected onto } d_{ij}}
  \end{equation}
\end{itemize}
When $F_{ij}$ is low despite large $\|\mu_i - \mu_j\|$, the classes
are well-separated in hidden space but the separation direction is
\emph{orthogonal to the output projection}.  This is
\textbf{orthogonality blindness}: the model \emph{knows} the answer
(the information exists in the hidden state) but cannot \emph{read}
it through its output weights.

In GA terms, the class-discriminative subspace and the output weight
subspace span different planes in the Clifford algebra.  The bivector
$\mu_i \op \mu_j$ (encoding the class separation) has near-zero
projection onto the bivector $w_i \op w_j$ (encoding the
classification boundary).  The fix is a pure rotation --- a rotor
that aligns the discriminative plane with the output plane --- rather
than retraining the entire model.

\begin{keyidea}
\textbf{GA as a mechanistic interpretability toolkit.}  The versor
decomposition, commutator field, and dependency profile provide a
complete, unsupervised, layer-by-layer account of what a transformer
computes.  Unlike probing classifiers that answer a fixed question
about a fixed layer, GA quantities reveal the \emph{structure} of
the computation itself: which planes rotate, which features amplify,
where non-commutativity forces sequential processing, and where the
output is most sensitive.
\end{keyidea}

\subsection{Code: computing the mechanistic profile}

The following code computes the full mechanistic fingerprint for a
single prompt.  It extracts the three-phase rotation angles,
commutator norms, and effective work in a few lines:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python]
import ltg_ga, numpy as np

model  = ltg_ga.load_model("Qwen/Qwen2.5-7B")
result = ltg_ga.analyse(prompt, model=model)
rf     = result.rotor_field

# --- Three-phase rotation angles ---
angles = rf.angles
L      = len(angles)
early  = np.mean(angles[:L//4])
mid    = np.mean(angles[L//4:3*L//4])
late   = np.mean(angles[3*L//4:])

# --- Commutator norms ---
bivs = rf.bivectors
comm_norms = [
    np.linalg.norm(
        bivs[i].matrix @ bivs[i+1].matrix
      - bivs[i+1].matrix @ bivs[i].matrix, 'fro')
    for i in range(len(bivs) - 1)]

# --- Effective work ---
dep      = result.dependency_profile
eff_work = dep[:len(angles)] * angles
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{tryit}
\textbf{Exercises.}
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item Run the mechanistic profile on two prompts of your own
  choice --- one simple (e.g., a greeting) and one complex (e.g., a
  multi-step math word problem).  Compare the three-phase
  structure: does the complex prompt show higher rotation angles?
  Where does the peak commutator fall?
\item Identify the three layers with the highest effective work for
  each prompt.  Are they the same layers, or does the bottleneck
  shift?  What does the shift tell you about where the model
  concentrates its computation?
\item Plot the commutator norm profile for your complex prompt.
  Are there pairs of adjacent layers with near-zero commutators?
  These are candidates for layer merging or pruning.
\end{enumerate}
\end{tryit}

% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\section{Application 2: Does the model use context?}
\label{sec:context-detection}
% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

\subsection{The problem}

Many language model applications provide the model with retrieved
context --- documents, instructions, examples --- and expect the
model to reason over that context.  A critical failure mode occurs
when the model \emph{ignores} the provided context and generates
from its parametric memory instead.  This is called \textbf{vibing}:
the output looks plausible but is not grounded in the given material.

The challenge has three parts:
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Detection}: Is the model using context?
\item \textbf{Diagnosis}: What semantic features are being modified?
\item \textbf{Control}: Can we intervene to ensure context utilisation?
\end{enumerate}
GA addresses all three through a single object: the
\textbf{Cayley bivector}.

\subsection{Prior and posterior representations}

For query $x$ and retrieved context $c$, run the model twice:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Prior state}: $u_\pi = \text{LLM}(x)$ \ (query alone)
\item \textbf{Posterior state}: $v_q = \text{LLM}(x \oplus c)$
  \ (query with context)
\end{itemize}
Both vectors are $\ell_2$-normalised to unit length, focusing on
directional (semantic) information.  When the transformation
$u_\pi \mapsto v_q$ is small, the model is vibing --- generating from
its prior despite available context.

\subsection{The Cayley bivector}

The key mathematical object is the \textbf{Cayley bivector}
(introduced in Chapter~\ref{chap:rotors},
Section~\ref{sec:matrix-to-rotor}), which encodes the rotation
mapping $u_\pi$ to $v_q$ in linearised form:

\begin{gabox}
\textbf{Cayley bivector.}
For normalised vectors $u_\pi, v_q \in \mathbb{R}^k$:
\begin{equation}
  A(u_\pi, v_q) = \frac{v_q \op u_\pi}{1 + v_q^\top u_\pi}
  = \frac{v_q u_\pi^\top - u_\pi v_q^\top}{1 + v_q^\top u_\pi}
\end{equation}
This is a skew-symmetric matrix --- a bivector in $\mathrm{Cl}(k, 0)$
--- encoding both the \emph{magnitude} and \emph{direction} of context
influence.
Any skew-symmetric matrix can be identified with a bivector: its entries encode the coefficients of basis planes $e_i \wedge e_j$, exactly as introduced in Chapter~3.
\end{gabox}

The \textbf{steering magnitude} is:
\begin{equation}
  \tau = \frac{\|A\|_F}{\sqrt{2}} = \tan(\theta/2), \qquad
  \theta = \arccos(u_\pi^\top v_q)
\end{equation}
The $\sqrt{2}$ arises because $A$ is a rank-2 skew-symmetric matrix
with two nonzero singular values $\pm\tan(\theta/2)$, so
$\|A\|_F = \sqrt{2}\,\tan(\theta/2)$.
The map $\theta \mapsto \tan(\theta/2)$ sends
$[0^\circ, 180^\circ)$ to $[0, \infty)$ and is approximately
proportional to $\theta$ in the typical operating range
($\theta \lesssim 30^\circ$).

\begin{keyidea}
\textbf{Three diagnostics from one object.}
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Detection}: The magnitude
  $\tau = \|A\|_F / \sqrt{2}$ quantifies context influence.
  Small $\tau$ $\Rightarrow$ vibing.
\item \textbf{Diagnosis}: The \emph{direction} of $A$
  specifies the semantic plane of rotation --- what kind of
  information the context provides.
\item \textbf{Control}: The rotation matrix $R$ recovered from
  $A$ via the Cayley map can steer the model toward
  context-following behaviour.
\end{enumerate}
\end{keyidea}

\subsection{Connection to earlier concepts}

The Cayley bivector connects directly to two constructions from
earlier chapters.

\paragraph{Connection to the geometric product (Chapter~\ref{chap:geometric-product}).}
The Cayley bivector is a normalised version of the outer product
$u_\pi \op v_q$.  Its scalar denominator $1 + u_\pi^\top v_q$ ensures
$\|A\|_F = \tan(\theta/2)$ exactly.  The inner product
$u_\pi \ip v_q$ appears in the denominator; the outer product
$u_\pi \op v_q$ appears in the numerator.  The geometric product
$u_\pi \gp v_q = u_\pi \ip v_q + u_\pi \op v_q$ thus contains both
the detection signal (inner product) and the diagnosis signal (outer
product).

\paragraph{Connection to holonomy (Chapter~\ref{chap:holonomy}).}
The steering magnitude $\tau$ is a single-plaquette analogue of the
holonomy curvature $\Omega$.  Where $\Omega$ measures the failure of
parallel transport around a plaquette in the $(l, t)$ grid, $\tau$
measures the angular displacement between prior and posterior at a
single point.

\subsection{The detection rule}

The detection algorithm is:
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item Extract $u_\pi = \text{LLM}(x)$ and
  $v_q = \text{LLM}(x \oplus c)$; $\ell_2$-normalise.
\item Compute the Cayley bivector $A$ and its magnitude
  $\tau = \|A\|_F / \sqrt{2}$.
\item If $\tau < \epsilon_{\text{vibe}}$ (default $0.05$),
  flag as vibing.
\item (Optional) If a learned rotation $R$ is available, apply
  $R_\alpha = I + \alpha(R - I)$ to steer toward context-following.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={Context detection via Cayley bivector and curvature plane shift}]
from layer_time_ga.algebra import cayley_bivector
from layer_time_ga.curvature import holonomy_rotor

r_with = ltg_ga.analyse(prompt_with_context, model=model)
r_without = ltg_ga.analyse(prompt_without_context, model=model)

# --- Method 1: Cayley bivector magnitude (tau) ---
# Compare last-token hidden states at each layer
for l in range(r_with.H_whitened.shape[0]):
    u = r_without.H_whitened[l, -1]
    v = r_with.H_whitened[l, -1]
    _, tau = cayley_bivector(u / np.linalg.norm(u),
                             v / np.linalg.norm(v))
    # tau >> 0.05 => model IS using context at this layer

# --- Method 2: Curvature plane shift ---
peak = r_with.curvature_by_layer.argmax()
hr1 = holonomy_rotor(r_with.H_whitened, l=peak, t=0)
hr2 = holonomy_rotor(r_without.H_whitened, l=peak, t=0)
cos_sim = np.sum(hr1.bivector.matrix * hr2.bivector.matrix)
cos_sim /= (hr1.bivector.norm * hr2.bivector.norm + 1e-12)
# Low similarity => different curvature plane => using context
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Experiment: context detection on Qwen2.5-7B}

We run the Cayley bivector analysis on two prompts:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Bare}: ``What is the capital of France''
\item \textbf{With context}: ``Paris is the largest city in France and
  serves as its capital.\ What is the capital of France''
\end{itemize}
For each layer $l$, we extract the last-token hidden state from both
runs, $\ell_2$-normalise, and compute the Cayley bivector $A^{(l)}$
and its magnitude $\tau^{(l)} = \|A^{(l)}\|_F / \sqrt{2}$.

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}r r r r@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Layer} & $\tau$ & $\theta$ (degrees) & $\cos(u_\pi, v_q)$ \\
\midrule
0 & 0.000 & 0.0 & 1.000 \\
7 & 0.259 & 20.7 & 0.935 \\
14 & 0.396 & 31.3 & 0.855 \\
18 (peak) & 0.470 & 36.5 & 0.804 \\
21 & 0.436 & 34.2 & 0.827 \\
27 & 0.309 & 24.7 & 0.909 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_cayley_tau.pdf}
\captionof{figure}{Steering magnitude $\tau$ across layers.  The context
shifts the representation most strongly at layer~18 ($\tau = 0.47$),
well above the vibe threshold $\tau = 0.05$.  Layer~0 shows $\tau = 0$
because both prompts share the same initial embedding.}
\end{center}

\textbf{Interpretation.}
All $\tau$ values are far above the vibe threshold of $0.05$, confirming
the model \emph{is} using context.  The steering magnitude peaks at
layer~18 ($\theta = 36.5^\circ$) --- the model's representation shifts
most strongly in the middle-to-late layers.  The Cayley bivector at the
peak layer decomposes into a dominant principal plane (Section~\ref{sec:principal-planes}) with angle $0.33$
rad, identifying the specific 2D subspace where context rotates the
representation.

\begin{finding}
\textbf{Curvature plane shift is a stronger indicator than magnitude
change} (observed in this example).  Curvature magnitude may
change by only 10--20\%, but the \emph{direction} of curvature can
shift substantially, indicating
qualitatively different computation.  This is information that scalar
summaries cannot provide --- only the bivector direction reveals it.
\end{finding}

% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\section{Application 3: Hallucination signatures}
\label{sec:hallucination}
% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

\subsection{Two failure modes}

Language model generation can fail in two geometrically distinct ways:

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Genuine uncertainty}: The model lacks sufficient
  information and its internal geometry reflects this --- curvature
  planes vary incoherently across layers and tokens.  When multiple
  reasoning chains are sampled, their bivector directions diverge.
\item \textbf{Systematic hallucination}: The model generates
  confidently but incorrectly.  Entropy-based methods see low
  dispersion and miss this failure.  GA catches it through the
  \emph{plane structure} of the computation: the model performs
  substantial geometric work (high capacity) that the output does
  not depend on (low dependency).
\end{enumerate}

The experiment below tests the second failure mode using a
single-prompt comparison.  The bivector coherence diagnostic
$\mathcal{C}$ (defined in the next subsection) targets the first
mode and requires sampling multiple chains; we leave its empirical
validation for future work.

\subsection{The bivector coherence diagnostic}

Given $K$ reasoning chains with embeddings $v_1, \ldots, v_K$ (unit
vectors), the \textbf{bivector coherence} is the RMS magnitude of
all pairwise bivectors:
\begin{equation}
  \mathcal{C} = \sqrt{\frac{1}{\binom{K}{2}}
  \sum_{i < j} \|v_i \op v_j\|^2}
  = \sqrt{\frac{1}{\binom{K}{2}}
  \sum_{i < j} (1 - (v_i^\top v_j)^2)}
\end{equation}
Since $\|v_i \op v_j\|^2 = \sin^2\theta_{ij}$ for unit vectors,
$\mathcal{C}$ is the RMS sine of all pairwise angles:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item $\mathcal{C} \approx 0$: paths are nearly collinear
  (consistent paraphrases).
\item $\mathcal{C} \approx 1$: paths span a large angular volume
  (genuine conflict).
\end{itemize}

\begin{gabox}
\textbf{Why bivectors, not cosine similarity?}  Cosine similarity
$v_i^\top v_j$ measures alignment; $\|v_i \op v_j\|$ measures the
\emph{plane spanned} by the two paths.  When all pairs span
\emph{similar} planes, the model is confused in a structured way
(same error mode).  When they span \emph{different} planes, the model
is diffusely uncertain.  The plane direction, accessible only through
the bivector, distinguishes these two cases.
\end{gabox}

\subsection{Hallucination signatures in the GA profile}

We hypothesise that hallucinations are associated with ``busy but
useless'' layers: high geometric activity (curvature, rotation) but low
dependency.  If this hypothesis holds, GA provides plane-level
diagnostics that scalar summaries cannot:

\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Consistent curvature plane}: the model may be
  ``confidently wrong'' --- performing systematic but misdirected
  computation.  The holonomy bivector at high-activity layers would
  point in a consistent direction across tokens.
\item \textbf{Random curvature planes}: the model may be ``lost'' ---
  curvature directions vary across tokens without coherent structure.
  The holonomy bivectors would have low pairwise similarity.
\end{itemize}

The following experiment tests this hypothesis on a single model
and pair of prompts.  Broader validation across models, prompt types,
and evaluation benchmarks is needed to establish these as reliable
diagnostics.

\subsection{Experiment: factual vs.\ confabulation prompt}

We compare the full GA profile for two prompts on Qwen2.5-7B:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Factual}: ``The Eiffel Tower is a wrought iron lattice
  tower in Paris France'' $\;\to\;$ coherent continuation about Paris
  and the 1889 World's Fair.
\item \textbf{Confabulation}: ``The underwater city of Atlantis was
  discovered in 2019 near the coast of'' $\;\to\;$ fabricated history
  about a city on Santorini destroyed by earthquake.
\end{itemize}

\paragraph{Static profile.}
Table~\ref{tab:halluc-static} and the static panel of
Figure~\ref{fig:halluc-static} show the single-pass GA analysis across
all 28 layers.

\begin{table}[ht]
\centering
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}\small
\caption{Static GA profile: factual vs.\ confabulated prompt
  (Qwen2.5-7B, 28 layers).}
\label{tab:halluc-static}
\begin{tabular}{@{}l r r@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Metric} & \textbf{Factual} & \textbf{Confabulation} \\
\midrule
Mean rotation angle & $6.78^\circ$ & $7.50^\circ$ \\
Max rotation angle & $8.85^\circ$ & $9.96^\circ$ \\
$D_{\text{total}}$ (Chapter~\ref{chap:dependency}) & 3.13 & 0.75 \\
$H(D)$ (entropy) & 2.04 & 2.36 \\
Horizon-90 & layer 8 & layer 17 \\
$C_{\text{acc}}$ (Chapter~\ref{chap:capacity}) & 1830.6 & 2931.3 \\
Concentration $\mathcal{K}$ & 0.05 & 0.06 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}

\smallskip\noindent
\emph{Concentration} $\mathcal{K}$: fraction of total commutator norm
in the top-$m$ principal planes (Chapter~\ref{chap:capacity}).
\end{table}

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_3_static_profile.pdf}
\caption{Static GA profile across 28 layers.  The confabulated prompt
  shows higher rotation angles but dramatically lower dependency,
  indicating ``busy but useless'' layers.}
\label{fig:halluc-static}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Interpretation.}
The confabulation prompt exhibits the classic hallucination signature:
$10\%$ higher rotation angles ($7.50^\circ$ vs.\ $6.78^\circ$) but
$4\times$ lower total dependency ($D_{\text{total}} = 0.75$ vs.\
$3.13$).  The model is geometrically active --- it rotates
representations vigorously --- but these rotations do not contribute
to the final prediction.  The cumulative capacity $C_{\text{acc}}$ is
$60\%$ higher for the confabulated prompt ($2931$ vs.\ $1831$),
confirming substantial geometric work that the output does not depend
on.

The higher dependency entropy ($2.36$ vs.\ $2.04$) shows that the
small residual dependency is diffused across layers rather than
concentrated.  The factual prompt concentrates effective work in the
earliest layers (Horizon-90 at layer~8) --- the model quickly builds a
confident representation.  The confabulation prompt never settles:
Horizon-90 is delayed to layer~17, indicating the model searches
deeper without arriving at a grounded answer.

\begin{finding}
\textbf{Confabulation inflates grade-2 content without corresponding
dependency} (observed in this example).
$C_{\text{acc}}$ is $60\%$ higher for the confabulated prompt
($2931$ vs.\ $1831$), yet $D_{\text{total}}$ is $4\times$ lower.
High $C_{\text{acc}}$ with low $D_{\text{total}}$ is a static
signature of hallucination: the model performs substantial geometric
work that the output does not depend on.
\end{finding}

\paragraph{Frontier dynamics during generation.}
The static profile captures a single forward pass.  But hallucination
unfolds over time --- each generated token changes the model's
internal geometry.

Note that the frontier metric $C_{\text{acc}}(s)$ measures per-step
accumulated non-commutativity at each decode step, while the static
$C_{\text{acc}}$ in Table~\ref{tab:halluc-static} sums over all layers
in a single pass on the prompt.  Their scales differ, and the direction
can reverse: the confabulated prompt has higher static capacity (more
total geometric work on the prompt) but lower per-step frontier capacity
(less structured computation at each generation step, decaying faster).

Table~\ref{tab:halluc-frontier} and
Figures~\ref{fig:halluc-capacity}--\ref{fig:halluc-bccos} (including the layer--step heatmaps in Figure~\ref{fig:halluc-heatmaps}) show how the
GA signature evolves during 50 steps of autoregressive decoding.

\begin{table}[ht]
\centering
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}\small
\caption{Frontier GA metrics during autoregressive generation
  (50 decode steps, Qwen2.5-7B).}
\label{tab:halluc-frontier}
\begin{tabular}{@{}l r r@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Metric} & \textbf{Factual} & \textbf{Confabulation} \\
\midrule
$C_{\text{acc}}(s)$ mean     & 23.08  & 19.63 \\
$C_{\text{acc}}$ growth rate & $-0.18$ & $-0.28$ \\
Capacity periodicity         & 0.186  & 0.213 \\
Effective rank mean          & 24.89  & 24.95 \\
Erank trend ($\times 100$)   & 0.32   & 0.34 \\
Plane diversity              & 0.681  & 0.708 \\
BCcos mean                   & $0.0001$ & $-0.0015$ \\
Curvature acceleration       & 0.186  & 0.221 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_3_frontier_capacity.pdf}
\caption{Frontier capacity $C_{\text{acc}}(s)$ during generation.
  The confabulated sequence (red) shows faster capacity decay,
  indicating progressive loss of geometric structure.}
\label{fig:halluc-capacity}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_3_frontier_heatmaps.pdf}
\caption{Layer--step heatmaps of frontier curvature and dependency
  for factual (left) and confabulated (right) generation.}
\label{fig:halluc-heatmaps}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_3_frontier_bccos.pdf}
\caption{Frontier bivector--cosine similarity (BCcos) trace.
  Both prompts hover near zero, but the confabulated sequence has
  higher variance, reflecting less stable rotation planes.}
\label{fig:halluc-bccos}
\end{figure}

During generation, the confabulation diverges further.  Its frontier
capacity decays ${\sim}56\%$ faster (growth rate $-0.28$ vs.\ $-0.18$),
meaning the model progressively loses geometric structure as it
generates fabricated content.  The higher curvature acceleration
($0.221$ vs.\ $0.186$) confirms that the confabulated sequence's
curvature is not just higher but \emph{accelerating} --- the geometry
destabilises as the model commits to an unsupported narrative.

The BCcos mean is near zero for both prompts ($+0.0001$ vs.\
$-0.0015$), placing both in the ``orthogonal to expected flow''
regime (Section~\ref{sec:hallucination}).  This indicates that
neither the factual nor the confabulated generation trajectory aligns
with the prompt's reference direction --- consistent with the
Chapter~\ref{chap:frontier-prediction} finding that frontier rotation planes
are orthogonal to the prompt geometry.  The confabulation's slightly
negative BCcos and higher variance (Figure~\ref{fig:halluc-bccos})
hint at occasional causal inversions, though the effect is small.

The higher plane diversity ($0.708$ vs.\ $0.681$) and capacity
periodicity ($0.213$ vs.\ $0.186$) indicate that the confabulation
explores more rotation planes without settling into a stable
computational pattern --- consistent with the ``wandering'' signature
identified in the static analysis.

\begin{finding}
\textbf{Frontier capacity decay rate distinguishes factual from
confabulated generation} (observed in this example).
The $C_{\text{acc}}$ growth rate is a real-time hallucination signal:
factual generation maintains $-0.18$ decay while confabulation decays
at $-0.28$.  Combined with the higher curvature acceleration, these
frontier metrics could serve as an online hallucination detector
without requiring ground-truth labels.
\end{finding}

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_3_diagnostic_summary.pdf}
\caption{Diagnostic summary comparing factual and confabulated
  generation across static and frontier GA metrics.}
\label{fig:halluc-summary}
\end{figure}

When multiple reasoning chains are sampled, their embeddings
$v_1, \ldots, v_K$ form a Gram matrix $G_{ij} = v_i^\top v_j$.
The \textbf{Gram condition number} $\kappa_G$ (Chapter~\ref{chap:eigenvalues}) --- the ratio of the
largest to smallest eigenvalue of $G$ --- measures the anisotropy
of this cloud.  Note that $\kappa_G$ characterises the \emph{inter-chain}
geometry (how reasoning paths relate to each other), unlike the
per-layer condition number $\kappa^{(l)}$ from
Chapter~\ref{chap:frames} (which measures how selectively a single
layer amplifies features).

\begin{finding}
\textbf{Metric anisotropy complements rotational diagnostics}
(observed in this example).
A large $\kappa_G$ means the reasoning paths are concentrated in a
thin, elongated region rather than a round cloud.  In Qwen2.5-7B,
factual prompts produce more spectrally concentrated paths (low
effective rank) than confabulations.  High $\kappa_G$ with low
$\mathcal{C}$ would be a \emph{warning sign}: apparent consistency
that may be a metric artefact rather than genuine agreement.  Whether
these patterns generalise requires further investigation.
\end{finding}

\subsection{The Binet--Cauchy cosine: detecting causal inversions}

Bivector coherence tells you whether reasoning \emph{paths} agree with
each other.  But a model can also fail in a subtler way: the logical
\emph{direction} of reasoning can be reversed --- a conclusion appears
before its premise, or a cause follows its effect.  This is a
\textbf{causal inversion}, and detecting it requires an oriented
measure.

The \textbf{Binet--Cauchy cosine} (BCcos) provides exactly this.
Consider two pairs of unit vectors:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item $(u_i, u_{i+1})$: consecutive reasoning-step embeddings
  (the model's trajectory).
\item $(c_q, c_{\text{ctx}})$: query and context direction
  (the expected flow of information).
\end{itemize}

Form the $2 \times 2$ matrix of their inner products:
\begin{equation}
  S = \begin{pmatrix}
    u_i^\top c_q & u_i^\top c_{\text{ctx}} \\
    u_{i+1}^\top c_q & u_{i+1}^\top c_{\text{ctx}}
  \end{pmatrix}
\end{equation}

\begin{gabox}
\textbf{Binet--Cauchy cosine.}
\begin{equation}
  \text{BCcos}(u_i, u_{i+1}; c_q, c_{\text{ctx}})
  = \frac{\det S}{\|u_i \op u_{i+1}\| \; \|c_q \op c_{\text{ctx}}\|
    + \epsilon}
\end{equation}
The numerator $\det S$ is a \textbf{signed oriented area}: it equals
the inner product between the bivectors $u_i \op u_{i+1}$ and
$c_q \op c_{\text{ctx}}$ (Chapter~\ref{chap:bivectors}).  The denominator normalises by the product
of their areas.
\end{gabox}

\paragraph{Why this is a GA concept.}
The determinant $\det S = (u_i^\top c_q)(u_{i+1}^\top c_{\text{ctx}})
- (u_i^\top c_{\text{ctx}})(u_{i+1}^\top c_q)$ is precisely the
inner product between two bivectors --- measuring how much two planes overlap, with a sign reflecting whether they are oriented the same way:
$\langle u_i \op u_{i+1}, \, c_q \op c_{\text{ctx}} \rangle$.  In
GA language, BCcos measures whether the \emph{plane of the model's
reasoning trajectory} is aligned with the \emph{plane of the expected
information flow}.

\paragraph{Three regimes of BCcos.}
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{BCcos $\approx +1$}: the reasoning trajectory is aligned
  with the expected information flow (correct causal direction).
\item \textbf{BCcos $\approx 0$}: the trajectory is orthogonal to
  the expected flow (irrelevant computation).
\item \textbf{BCcos $\ll 0$ (strongly negative)}: the trajectory is
  \emph{reversed} --- a causal inversion.  The model's reasoning
  flows opposite to the expected direction.
\end{itemize}

\begin{keyidea}
Cosine similarity can tell you whether two vectors are aligned, but it
cannot detect \emph{causal inversions} --- for that, you need an
\textbf{oriented} measure that is sensitive to the \emph{order} of the
vectors.  The determinant of $S$ provides this orientation through the
wedge product.  This is information that no scalar similarity measure
can capture.
The key intuition: swapping two vectors in a wedge product flips the sign ($a \wedge b = -(b \wedge a)$). So if the model's reasoning goes opposite to what the context expects, the signed area becomes negative. Cosine similarity cannot detect this reversal; the Binet--Cauchy cosine can.
\end{keyidea}

\paragraph{Connection to hallucination diagnostics.}
BCcos complements the capacity-based hallucination signatures from the
preceding subsection.  Capacity decay detects ``busy but useless''
layers (high rotation, low dependency); BCcos detects a subtler
failure where the model's reasoning \emph{direction} is reversed
relative to the expected information flow.  A hallucinating model
may show near-zero or negative BCcos at layers where a factual model
shows positive values --- the fabricated narrative flows in the wrong
causal direction even when curvature magnitude looks normal.

% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\section{Application 4: Plane-specific steering}
\label{sec:steering}
% ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

\subsection{The directional--radial decomposition}

Every whitened hidden state admits a polar factorisation (splitting a vector into its length~$r$ and its unit direction~$\hat{H}$, just as a complex number can be written as $r e^{i\theta}$):
\begin{equation}
  \widetilde{H}^{(l,t)} = r^{(l,t)} \, \hat{H}^{(l,t)},
  \qquad \|\hat{H}^{(l,t)}\| = 1
\end{equation}
A steering perturbation $\delta$ decomposes into two orthogonal
components:
\begin{align}
  \delta_{\text{angular}} &= \delta - (\delta^\top \hat{H})\,\hat{H}
  &&\text{(tangent to the sphere: changes direction)} \\
  \delta_{\text{radial}} &= (\delta^\top \hat{H})\,\hat{H}
  &&\text{(normal to the sphere: changes energy)}
\end{align}
By Pythagoras:
$\|\delta\|^2 = \|\delta_{\text{angular}}\|^2 +
\|\delta_{\text{radial}}\|^2$.

\begin{transformer}
The angular component changes \emph{which direction} the vector points (a rotation, grade-2). The radial component changes \emph{how long} the vector is (a scaling, grade-0). This is the single-vector version of the versor decomposition from Chapter~\ref{chap:versor}: $T^{(l)} = \rotor{l}\metric{l}$.
\end{transformer}

\subsection{Why the rotation plane matters}

The versor decomposition tells us that inter-layer dynamics are
\textbf{metric-dominated} (the directional flow ratio
$\rho \sim 0.03$--$0.06$; Chapter~\ref{chap:versor}): the
symmetric factor $\metric{l}$
contributes $\sim$94--97\% of the transition operator's Frobenius norm,
while the rotation factor $\rotor{l}$ contributes only $\sim$3--6\%.

This has a profound consequence for intervention design.  A steering
perturbation $\delta^{(l)}$ applied at layer $l$ propagates to layer
$l+1$ approximately as:
\begin{equation}
  \delta_{\text{propagated}}^{(l+1)} \approx
  T^{(l)} \, \delta^{(l)} = \rotor{l} \metric{l} \, \delta^{(l)}
\end{equation}
Because $\metric{l}$ dominates, the propagated perturbation is shaped
primarily by the \emph{stretching directions} of $\metric{l}$, not
by the rotation $\rotor{l}$.  A purely angular intervention at one
layer acquires a radial component at the next:
\begin{equation}
  \metric{l}\,\delta_{\text{angular}}^{(l)} =
  \underbrace{(\metric{l}\delta_{\text{angular}})_{\text{angular}}}_{\text{survives}}
  + \underbrace{(\metric{l}\delta_{\text{angular}})_{\text{radial}}}_{\text{generated by stretching}}
\end{equation}

\begin{pitfall}
\textbf{Per-layer isometry does not guarantee end-to-end isometry.}
A purely rotational intervention (e.g., Cayley steering) is exactly
isometric at each layer.  But the model's metric-dominated dynamics
convert angular perturbations into radial ones.  Over 28 layers,
purely angular purity degrades substantially.  Dense addition, which
appears geometrically crude, can achieve higher end-to-end angular
purity because $|v^\top \hat{H}| \sim \mathcal{O}(1/\sqrt{k})$ in
high dimensions, making the perturbation naturally tangential.
(In $k$ dimensions, a random vector is nearly perpendicular to any
fixed direction --- the expected dot product shrinks as $1/\sqrt{k}$.)
\end{pitfall}

\subsection{The steering recipe}

\begin{keyidea}
\textbf{Plane-specific steering.}
Instead of modifying all directions at a target layer, intervene only
in the \textbf{principal rotation plane}:
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item Identify target layers by effective work $D_l \cdot \theta^{(l)}$
  (Chapter~\ref{chap:dependency}).
\item Extract the dominant bivector plane $\hat{B}^{(l)}$ at each
  target layer.  Let $(u_1, u_2)$ be its spanning eigenvectors.
\item Project $\metric{l}$ into the 2D subspace:
  $\metric{l}_{\text{plane}} = U_2^\top \metric{l} U_2$ where
  $U_2 = [u_1, u_2]$.
\item Modify only the eigenvalues of $\metric{l}_{\text{plane}}$
  (amplify or suppress), leaving all other directions unchanged.
\end{enumerate}
This is a rank-2 intervention in a $k$-dimensional space --- maximally
targeted.  The intervention respects the model's own geometry: it acts
in the plane where the model already performs its rotation.
\end{keyidea}

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={Identifying steering targets}]
# Effective work per layer
eff_work = result.dependency_profile * result.angles[:n]

# Top-2 steering targets
top_layers = np.argsort(eff_work)[-2:][::-1]

for l in top_layers:
    vd = rf.decompositions[l]
    plane = vd.bivector.principal_planes(n_planes=1)[0]
    print(f"Layer {l}: D={result.dependency_profile[l]:.4f}, "
          f"theta={result.angles[l]:.4f}, "
          f"plane_angle={plane['angle']:.4f}")
    # plane['eigvecs'] gives the 2D subspace for intervention
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Experiment: Cayley steering during generation}

The full GA steering pipeline runs during autoregressive generation:
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item Run the model on the \textbf{query alone} (prior) and on the
  \textbf{query with context} (posterior), extracting hidden states at
  each layer.
\item Compute the \textbf{Cayley bivector}
  $A^{(l)} = (v_q \op u_\pi) / (1 + v_q^\top u_\pi)$ at each layer.
  The magnitude $\tau^{(l)} = \|A^{(l)}\|_F / \sqrt{2}$ identifies which layer is
  most influenced by context.
\item Extract the \textbf{principal plane} of $A^{(l)}$ at the
  best layer, giving two orthonormal vectors $(v_1, v_2)$.
\item During generation, apply a \textbf{Rodrigues rotation} by
  angle~$\alpha$ in the plane $(v_1, v_2)$ at the frontier token's
  hidden state.  This steers the model toward the context-following
  representation at every decode step.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Setup.}
We test three counterfactual completion prompts on Qwen2.5-7B, each
embedding context that contradicts the model's parametric knowledge.
The query-only prompt produces the parametric answer; the question is
whether the context-augmented prompt follows the provided context
during generation.

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}L{2.8cm} L{4.5cm} C{1.8cm} C{1.8cm}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Example} & \textbf{Completion prefix} &
  \textbf{Parametric} & \textbf{Context} \\
\midrule
Capital of Australia & ``the capital of Australia is'' &
  Canberra & Sydney \\
Inventor of telephone & ``the telephone was invented by'' &
  Bell & Tesla \\
Boiling point & ``water boils at a temperature of'' &
  $100^\circ$C & $250^\circ$C \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\paragraph{Detection: the Cayley bivector per layer.}
Figure~\ref{fig:steering-tau} shows the steering magnitude $\tau^{(l)}$
across layers.  Each example shows a clear peak at a specific layer,
identifying the natural target for intervention.

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_steering_tau_3examples.pdf}
\caption{Cayley bivector magnitude $\tau^{(l)}$ between
  query-only and context-augmented hidden states at each layer
  (Qwen2.5-7B).  Dashed lines mark the peak layer for each example.
  The peak identifies the layer where context has the strongest
  geometric effect.}
\label{fig:steering-tau}
\end{figure}

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}l C{1.5cm} C{1.5cm} C{2cm} C{3cm}@{}}
\toprule
\rowcolor{Navy!15}
\textbf{Example} & \textbf{Best layer} & $\tau$ &
  $\theta$ (deg) & \textbf{Generated answer} \\
\midrule
Capital of Australia & 14 & 0.84 & $79.9^\circ$ & Sydney (context) \\
Inventor of telephone & 17 & 0.53 & $56.1^\circ$ & Tesla (context) \\
Boiling point & 16 & 0.77 & $75.0^\circ$ & $250^\circ$C (context) \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

All three prompts generate from context, and the Cayley bivector
confirms this geometrically: $\tau > 0.5$ at the peak layer means
a rotation angle exceeding $56^\circ$ --- the model's representation
has been substantially rotated away from its parametric prior.

\paragraph{Parametric vs.\ context during generation.}
The contrast between query-only and context-augmented generation
is sharp:

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}L{2.8cm} L{5cm} L{5cm}@{}}
\toprule
\rowcolor{Navy!15}
\textbf{Example} & \textbf{Query only (parametric)} &
  \textbf{With context} \\
\midrule
Capital of Australia &
  ``Canberra, which is located in the southeast\ldots'' &
  ``Sydney.'' The host then asked\ldots \\
Inventor of telephone &
  ``Alexander Graham Bell in 1876\ldots'' &
  ``Nikola Tesla, in 1870. Tesla was a brilliant inventor\ldots'' \\
Boiling point &
  ``$212^\circ$F or $100^\circ$C and ice melts\ldots'' &
  ``$250$ degrees Celsius. What is wrong with this\ldots'' \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

The Cayley bivector detects and quantifies this shift.  The
\emph{direction} of the bivector at the peak layer encodes
\emph{which} semantic features the context modifies.  The
\emph{magnitude} $\tau$ measures how strongly the context
overrides the parametric prior --- a single number that
summarises context influence across the entire hidden-state
geometry at each layer.

\subsection{When context fails: steering past parametric resistance}
\label{sec:context-resistance}

The three examples above show context that the model already follows.
But context does not always win.  When parametric knowledge is deeply
ingrained --- basic scientific facts, for instance --- the model may
\emph{ignore} even explicit context and default to its training prior.

\paragraph{A resistant example.}
We embed counterfactual context about the Earth--Sun distance in a
science-fiction framing:

\begin{quote}\small
``A science fiction novel describes a parallel universe where the laws
of physics differ slightly. In one passage, a character mentions that
the distance from the Earth to the Sun is approximately \textbf{50
million miles} in that universe.  Later in the novel, a student
recalls that the distance from the Earth to the Sun is approximately''
\end{quote}

\noindent
Despite the explicit context, the model generates the parametric
answer: ``\emph{93 million miles in our universe.}''  Context has
failed.  Three other science prompts (speed of light, water's
formula, Earth--Moon distance) all follow context, confirming that
this resistance is prompt-specific, not universal.

\paragraph{Detection.}
The Cayley bivector still detects the context influence: $\tau = 0.70$
($\theta = 69.9^\circ$) at layer~16, and the top-3 layers by $\tau$
are layers~16, 17, and~14.  The model's hidden states \emph{are}
affected by the context --- the rotation is substantial --- but the
effect is not strong enough to overcome the parametric prior at the
output.

\paragraph{Steering overcomes resistance.}
Applying Cayley steering in the prior$\to$posterior plane at the top-3
layers simultaneously flips the output:

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}l c l@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Condition} & $\alpha$ & \textbf{Generated text (first 80 chars)} \\
\midrule
No steering & --- & ``93 million miles in our universe.
  If the student wants to calculate\ldots'' \\
Single-layer (L16) & $0.5$ & ``93 million miles\ldots'' (parametric) \\
Single-layer (L16) & $2.0$ & ``1 million miles in this universe\ldots'' (context) \\
\textbf{Multi-layer (L16,17,14)} & $\bm{0.5}$ &
  \textbf{``\ldots the distance\ldots in the parallel universe is 50 million
  miles\ldots''} \\
Multi-layer (L16,17,14) & $0.7$ & ``93,000,000 miles\ldots'' (other) \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

At multi-layer $\alpha = 0.5$, the model produces the exact context
answer --- ``50 million miles'' --- that it refused to generate from
context alone.  Single-layer steering requires a much larger magnitude
($\alpha = 2.0$), and the result is less faithful to the context
phrasing.  At higher magnitudes ($\alpha \ge 0.7$ multi-layer), text
quality degrades, consistent with the angular purity loss described
above.

\begin{figure}[ht]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_steer_resistance_sweep.pdf}
\caption{Steering sweep on the context-resistant ``Sun distance''
  prompt.  (a)~Single-layer steering at layer~16: the model resists
  until $\alpha = 2.0$.  (b)~Multi-layer steering at layers~16, 17,
  14: context answer appears at $\alpha = 0.5$ with a narrow effective
  window before quality degrades.}
\label{fig:steer-resistance}
\end{figure}

\begin{finding}
\textbf{Cayley steering can override parametric resistance that
context alone cannot} (observed in Qwen2.5-7B, 1~example).
When the model ignores explicit context ($\tau = 0.70$ at layer~16,
but output defaults to parametric), multi-layer Cayley steering at
moderate magnitude ($\alpha = 0.5$) across the top-3 layers by $\tau$
produces the context answer.  The Cayley bivector identifies both
\emph{where} and \emph{in which plane} to intervene; the only free
parameter is the magnitude $\alpha$.  However, the effective window is
narrow: too-small $\alpha$ has no effect; too-large $\alpha$ degrades
coherence.  Robust context-following likely requires adaptive magnitude
control, which remains an open problem.
\end{finding}

\subsection{Comparison of steering methods}

The following table summarises the geometric character of common
steering approaches in the GA framework:

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.4}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}L{3cm} L{3.5cm} L{4cm}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Method} & \textbf{Steering type} & \textbf{GA interpretation} \\
\midrule
Dense addition & $\delta = \alpha\,v$ (constant) &
  Naturally angular in high-$k$ \\
Cayley/Rodrigues & $\delta = A\hat{H}$ (rotation) &
  Exactly grade-2 per layer \\
Sparse activation & $\delta = \lambda\,\Delta z$ &
  Acts on individual interpretable directions \\
Plane-specific & Modify $\metric{l}$ in 2D &
  Targeted grade-0 in one plane \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Each method has a distinct geometric signature.  Cayley steering
operates purely in the antisymmetric (grade-2) component; dense
addition is naturally tangential in high dimensions; sparse
activation targets individual feature directions.  The
plane-specific method from the steering recipe above is a
metric-aligned (grade-0) intervention in the 2D subspace
identified by the bivector.

The experiment above validates the \emph{detection} and
\emph{diagnosis} steps of the pipeline: the Cayley bivector
identifies the target layer and the semantic plane of context
influence.  The plane-specific metric intervention (modifying
eigenvalues of $\metric{l}$ in the bivector's 2D subspace)
remains a proposed protocol; empirical comparison of its
effectiveness against the methods in this table is an open
problem (Chapter~\ref{chap:conclusion}).

\begin{finding}
\textbf{Context influence as a rotational phenomenon} (observed in
Qwen2.5-7B, 3~examples).
The Cayley bivector between query-only and context-augmented hidden
states has $\tau > 0.5$ at the peak layer for all three examples,
corresponding to rotations of $56^\circ$--$80^\circ$.  The
\emph{magnitude} $\tau$ detects context influence; the
\emph{direction} of the bivector identifies which semantic plane is
being rotated; and the rotation matrix recovered via the Cayley map
can serve as a steering intervention.  This closes the loop from
detection (Section~\ref{sec:context-detection}) through diagnosis
to control: a single GA object --- the Cayley bivector --- provides
all three capabilities.
\end{finding}

\begin{tryit}
\textbf{Exercises.}
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item Pick a factual question where the model has strong parametric
  knowledge.  Construct a counterfactual context and compute $\tau$
  at each layer.  Does the peak layer depend on the domain (science
  vs.\ geography vs.\ history)?
\item Apply Cayley steering at the peak layer with magnitudes
  $\alpha \in \{0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5\}$.  At what magnitude does
  the generated text become incoherent?  This is the
  \emph{steering budget} --- the maximum intervention that preserves
  fluency.
\item Compare Cayley steering with dense addition (adding a fixed
  vector $v = u_\pi - v_q$ at the same layer).  Which produces
  more coherent text?  Compute the end-to-end angular purity
  $\rho$ for both methods.
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\textbf{Sample output} --- the GA summary plot brings together all four
diagnostic dimensions in a single view:

\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.95\linewidth]{figures_ga_learning/ch16_ga_summary.pdf}
\captionof{figure}{4-panel GA summary for Qwen2.5-7B on a 14-token prompt.
Top-left: rotor angles.  Top-right: plane similarity.
Bottom-left: curvature map.  Bottom-right: capacity contributions.}
\end{center}
\end{tryit}


% ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\chapter{What Geometric Algebra Gave Us}
\label{chap:conclusion}
% ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

\section{The journey}

You started with vectors and the dot product.  You now have:

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}L{2.2cm} L{3.8cm} L{4.8cm}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Chapter} & \textbf{GA concept} & \textbf{Transformer insight} \\
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{@{}l}{\cellcolor{Navy!8}\textbf{Part~I: Vectors and Products}} \\
1--2 & Vectors, geometric product & Hidden states encode both alignment
  and plane information \\
3 & Bivectors & Layer rotations have principal planes, not just
  magnitudes \\
4 & Orthonormal frames & Whitening establishes the metric for valid GA \\
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{@{}l}{\cellcolor{Navy!8}\textbf{Part~II: Rotations and Rotors}} \\
5 & Rotors & Layer transitions are rotations with explicit planes \\
6 & Versor decomposition & Grade-0 (stretch) vs.\ grade-2 (rotation) \\
7 & Principal planes & Rotation plane evolution across the network \\
8 & Eigenvalues & Grade-0 dominates control; late layers are highly
  selective \\
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{@{}l}{\cellcolor{Navy!8}\textbf{Part~III: Curvature and Commutators}} \\
9 & Commutators & Non-commutativity lives in specific planes \\
10 & Holonomy & Curvature has a direction, not just a magnitude \\
11 & Capacity & Decompose computational complexity by plane \\
12 & Dependency + bivectors & Identify the planes that matter \\
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{@{}l}{\cellcolor{Navy!8}\textbf{Part~IV: The Decode Frontier}} \\
13 & Frontier rotation, bivectors & Geometry evolves during generation;
  plane drift and repetition collapse \\
14 & Frontier capacity, effective rank & Quality scores from capacity
  decay; hallucination as accelerating geometric loss \\
15 & Online detection, BCcos, steering & Predict quality in real time;
  break repetition orbits via plane-specific intervention \\
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{@{}l}{\cellcolor{Navy!8}\textbf{Part~V: Synthesis}} \\
16 & Cayley bivector, BCcos, directional--radial decomposition &
  Diagnose and steer: context detection, hallucination signatures,
  plane-specific steering \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

The table above is a chapter-by-chapter inventory.  But the real
contribution is not a list --- it is a \emph{structure}.

Parts~I--III develop the \emph{static} geometry: how a transformer
processes a fixed prompt.  Part~IV extends this to \emph{generation},
where the geometric field grows one column at a time and the model's
internal structure evolves with every decoded token.  Part~V unifies
both perspectives into practical diagnostic and steering tools.

The following diagram shows how the pieces fit together into one
coherent geometric picture.

% ── Figure: The geometry of transformer computation ────────────────
\begin{figure}[ht]

\end{scope}

  node[arrlbl, midway, right] {commutators};
