  inner sep=5pt, font=\small, text width=3.8cm, align=center]
  at (0,0) {%
  $H : (l,t) \to \mathbb{R}^k$\\[2pt]
  $R : (l,t) \to \mathrm{Spin}(k)$\\[2pt]
  $\Omega : (l,t) \to \mathfrak{so}(k)$\\[4pt]
  {\footnotesize\textbf{vectors\,,\;rotations\,,\;curvature}}};

\end{tikzpicture}

\caption{The geometric structure of transformer computation.
Hidden states form a vector field $H(l,t)$ over a grid of layers and
token positions.  Relationships between vectors are captured by the
geometric product, which decomposes into scalar (alignment) and
bivector (plane) components.  Each layer induces a
position-dependent transformation expressed as a rotor field
$R^{(l)}(t)$.  When these transformations do not commute across
directions, parallel transport around loops produces holonomy,
captured by the curvature bivector $\Omega(l,t)$.  Together, these
elements describe the transformer as a geometric system evolving
across both depth and context.}
\label{fig:master-geometry}
\end{figure}

\begin{keyidea}
This framework unifies the analysis of transformers into a single
geometric picture.  Instead of viewing the model as a sequence of
matrix operations, we interpret it as a field of vectors evolving
under local rotations, with global structure revealed through curvature.
The grid provides the domain, bivectors reveal directional structure,
rotors describe transformations, and holonomy measures interaction
between directions.  This perspective exposes structure that is invisible
to scalar summaries and traditional linear analysis.

\medskip
\centering
\emph{Learning = discovering geometric structure in the hidden-state field.}
\end{keyidea}


% ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
\section{The transformer as a discrete geometric field theory}
\label{sec:field-theory}
% ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

The observations throughout this book --- hidden-state grids, bivector
decompositions, rotor fields, holonomy --- are not independent facts.
They are elements of a single structure.  This section formalises
that structure.

\subsection{The base space: a discrete lattice}

Define the grid
\begin{equation}
  \mathcal{G} = \{(l,t) \mid l = 0,\dots,L-1,\; t = 0,\dots,T-1\}.
\end{equation}
The vertical axis indexes layers (depth, computation time); the
horizontal axis indexes token positions (sequence context).
$\mathcal{G}$ is the \textbf{base space} of the system.

\subsection{The fiber: hidden-state space}

At each grid point, the model maintains a vector
$H(l,t) \in \mathbb{R}^k$ (after whitening, so the metric is
Euclidean).  Thus the transformer defines a \textbf{vector field}:
\begin{equation}
  H : \mathcal{G} \longrightarrow \mathbb{R}^k.
\end{equation}
In the language of differential geometry, this is a section of a
trivial fiber bundle with base $\mathcal{G}$ and fiber
$\mathbb{R}^k$.

\subsection{Local transformations as a connection}

Moving across the grid changes the hidden state.  Each direction
has its own transport operator.

\paragraph{Vertical direction (layers).}
A layer induces a transformation
$U^{(l)}(t) : H(l,t) \to H(l+1,t)$,
locally approximated as
\begin{equation}
  U^{(l)}(t) \;\approx\; \exp\!\big(B^{(l)}(t)\big),
  \qquad B^{(l)}(t) \in \mathfrak{so}(k).
\end{equation}
The generator $B^{(l)}(t)$ is a skew-symmetric operator --- a
bivector encoding the rotation plane and magnitude.

\paragraph{Horizontal direction (tokens).}
Attention induces transformations across token positions:
$T^{(l)}(t) : H(l,t) \to H(l,t+1)$,
locally approximated as
\begin{equation}
  T^{(l)}(t) \;\approx\; \exp\!\big(C^{(l)}(t)\big),
  \qquad C^{(l)}(t) \in \mathfrak{so}(k).
\end{equation}

Together, $U^{(l)}$ and $T^{(l)}$ define a \textbf{connection} on the
grid: they prescribe how vectors are transported along vertical and
horizontal directions.

\subsection{Rotor field representation}

Using GA, the local transformations become rotors:
\begin{equation}
  R^{(l)}(t) = \exp\!\left(-\tfrac{1}{2}\, B^{(l)}(t)\right),
  \qquad
  H(l+1,t) \;\approx\; R^{(l)}(t)\; H(l,t)\; \widetilde{R}^{(l)}(t),
\end{equation}
where $\widetilde{R}$ is the reverse (inverse for rotors).  The
map $R^{(l)} : t \mapsto \mathrm{Spin}(k)$ is a field from token
positions to the spin group: a transformer layer defines a
\emph{field of rotations}, not a single transformation.

\subsection{Curvature from non-commutativity}

Consider a loop on the grid:
$(l,t) \to (l,t\!+\!1) \to (l\!+\!1,t\!+\!1) \to (l\!+\!1,t) \to (l,t)$.
The loop operator is
\begin{equation}
  R_{\text{loop}}(l,t) \;=\;
  R_{\uparrow}^{(t+1)}\;\;
  R_{\rightarrow}^{(l+1)}\;\;
  \big(R_{\uparrow}^{(t)}\big)^{-1}\;\;
  \big(R_{\rightarrow}^{(l)}\big)^{-1}.
\end{equation}
If the vertical and horizontal transformations commute,
$R_{\text{loop}} = I$ and the grid is flat.  Otherwise the
\textbf{curvature bivector}
\begin{equation}
  \Omega(l,t) = \log R_{\text{loop}}(l,t) \;\in\; \mathfrak{so}(k)
\end{equation}
is non-zero.  For small transformations, the holonomy is governed by
the commutator:
\begin{equation}
  R_{\text{loop}} \;\approx\; \exp\!\big([B^{(l)}, C^{(l)}]\big),
  \qquad
  \Omega(l,t) \;\approx\; [B^{(l)}(t),\; C^{(l)}(t)].
\end{equation}

\begin{gabox}
\textbf{Curvature arises from non-commutativity of transformations.}
When layer and token operations commute, curvature is zero and the
computation is separable.  When they do not commute, curvature
appears --- and its bivector reveals \emph{which plane} of
representation space is curved.
\end{gabox}

\subsection{Interpretation for transformer computation}

This framework yields five concrete re-interpretations:

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Tokens are positions} in a structured space, not
  independent objects.
\item \textbf{Hidden states are fields} --- contextualised vectors
  evolving across the grid.
\item \textbf{Layers are local transformations} --- position-dependent,
  not uniform.
\item \textbf{Attention induces horizontal transport} --- moving
  information across token positions.
\item \textbf{Reasoning corresponds to curvature} --- it appears when
  updates cannot be decomposed into independent layer and token steps.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Summary of the formal structure}

The full framework is captured in three fields:
\begin{equation}
\begin{aligned}
  H &: \mathcal{G} \;\longrightarrow\; \mathbb{R}^k
    &&\text{(hidden-state field)} \\[3pt]
  R &: \mathcal{G} \;\longrightarrow\; \mathrm{Spin}(k)
    &&\text{(rotor field)} \\[3pt]
  \Omega &: \mathcal{G} \;\longrightarrow\; \mathfrak{so}(k)
    &&\text{(curvature field)}
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}

\begin{keyidea}
A transformer is a discrete geometric field system whose computation
is governed by rotations and curvature.  The grid provides the domain,
bivectors reveal directional structure, rotors describe
transformations, and holonomy measures interaction between directions.
\end{keyidea}

\section{What was invisible before}

Scalar summaries --- the size of a rotation ($\|U - I\|_F$), the size
of a commutator ($\|[A_i, A_j]\|_F$), or the curvature magnitude
($\|R_{\text{loop}} - I\|_F$) --- tell you \emph{how much}.  GA tells you
\emph{how much} and \emph{in which direction}.  Specifically:

\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item Which planes each layer rotates in (Part~II).
\item Which planes the curvature acts in (Part~III).
\item Which planes different layers fail to commute in (Part~III).
\item Which planes the model's output depends on (Part~III).
\item How rotation planes drift, stabilise, or collapse during
  generation (Part~IV).
\item Whether frontier capacity decay is accelerating --- a real-time
  signal of hallucination (Parts~IV--V).
\item Whether the model's reasoning trajectory is causally aligned
  with its context, via the oriented Binet--Cauchy cosine (Part~V).
\end{enumerate}

Items 1--4 are the \emph{static} contribution: directional information
from a single forward pass that norms cannot provide.  Items 5--7 are
the \emph{dynamic} contribution: the decode frontier extends GA from
post-hoc analysis to real-time monitoring during generation.

\section{Where to go next}

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Frontier hallucination detection at scale.}  Our
  hallucination experiment (Section~\ref{sec:hallucination}) tested
  one model and one prompt pair.  The frontier capacity decay rate
  ($-0.28$ for confabulation vs.\ $-0.18$ for factual) is a
  promising online signal, but validating it as a reliable detector
  requires systematic evaluation across models, prompt types, and
  hallucination benchmarks.

\item \textbf{Online steering during generation.}  We demonstrated
  orbit-breaking via plane-specific intervention at a single decode
  step.  Can frontier-guided steering be applied continuously ---
  monitoring the capacity decay rate and intervening whenever it
  exceeds a threshold --- to prevent hallucination mid-generation?

\item \textbf{Higher grades.}  We used only grades 0 and 2.  Do
  trivectors (grade 3) carry meaningful information about three-layer
  interactions?

\item \textbf{Conformal GA.}  Conformal GA extends the algebra by
  adding two extra dimensions (written $\mathrm{Cl}(k+1,1)$), which
  allows translations --- not just rotations --- to be represented
  algebraically.  Could this unify residual connections (translations)
  with rotations (rotors)?

\item \textbf{GA in training dynamics.}  How does the bivector field
  evolve during pre-training?  Do specific planes emerge at specific
  training stages?

\item \textbf{Multimodal models.}  When vision and language tokens
  interact, do they occupy different rotation planes?

\item \textbf{Projective GA.}  Projective GA (written
  $\mathrm{Cl}(k,0,1)$) adds one extra dimension whose square is
  zero, providing a natural way to represent the affine shifts that
  residual connections add.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Further reading}

\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item Doran and Lasenby, \emph{Geometric Algebra for Physicists}
  (Cambridge, 2003).  The most accessible GA introduction.
\item Dorst, Fontijne, and Mann, \emph{Geometric Algebra for Computer
  Science} (Morgan Kaufmann, 2007).  Practical and code-oriented.
\item Lounesto, \emph{Clifford Algebras and Spinors} (Cambridge, 2001).
  The mathematical reference.
\item Sudjianto and Zhang, \emph{Layer--Time Geometry: How Language
  Models Process Information} (2026).  The first-edition linear algebra
  treatment.
\item Fang, Li, and Sudjianto, \emph{Design and Modeling for Computer
  Experiments} (Chapman \& Hall/CRC, 2006).  Background on DOE
  methodology.
\end{itemize}


% ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
% APPENDICES
% ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\appendix


% ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\chapter{Transformers: A Primer}
\label{app:transformers}
% ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

This appendix provides the transformer background needed for the book.
No prior ML knowledge is assumed; we build from first principles.

Every model we study in this book --- Qwen2.5-7B, Qwen2.5-32B,
DeepSeek-R1-Distill-Qwen-7B --- is a \textbf{GPT-style}
(decoder-only, autoregressive) transformer.  This is the architecture
behind GPT-4, Claude, LLaMA, and most modern large language models.
We describe this specific architecture below; encoder-only models
(like BERT) and encoder-decoder models (like the original Transformer
of Vaswani et~al., 2017) share many ideas but differ in structure.

\section{What is a language model?}

A language model is a function that, given a sequence of words (tokens),
predicts the next word.  ``The capital of France is'' $\to$ ``Paris.''
Internally, the model converts each token into a vector, transforms
those vectors through many layers of computation, and reads out the
prediction from the final layer.  The key objects for us are the
\textbf{hidden states} --- the intermediate vectors at each layer.

\section{Tokens and embeddings}

Before processing, text is split into \textbf{tokens} (roughly
word-pieces).  Each token is assigned a vector $H^{(0,t)} \in
\mathbb{R}^p$ from a learned \textbf{embedding table}.  The integer
$t$ indexes the token position; $p$ is the \textbf{hidden dimension}
(e.g., $p = 3584$ for Qwen2.5-7B).  These initial vectors are the
starting point for all subsequent computation.

\section{The embedding layer (layer 0)}

The embedding table is a matrix $W_E \in \mathbb{R}^{p \times V}$
where $V$ is the vocabulary size.  Each token $x_t$ (an integer index
into the vocabulary) is mapped to a column of this matrix:
\begin{equation}
  H^{(0,t)} = W_E[\,x_t\,] + \mathrm{pos}(t)
\end{equation}
where $\mathrm{pos}(t)$ is a \textbf{positional encoding} that tells
the model where in the sequence this token appears.  (Modern models
like Qwen use \emph{Rotary Position Embeddings} (RoPE), which inject
position information inside the attention mechanism rather than by
adding a vector here; the effect is similar.)

The key point for this book: \textbf{the embedding vectors
$H^{(0,t)}$ are not random.}  Tokens with related meanings have
similar embeddings (``cat'' and ``kitten'' are close, ``cat'' and
``democracy'' are far apart).  However, the embedding space is
organised very differently from the model's internal processing
space.  The transition from layer~0 to layer~1 is therefore
exceptionally large --- in Chapter~6, we observe a rotation of
$4.28$ radians ($245^\circ$) at this boundary, far larger than any
subsequent layer transition.  The first few layers are essentially
\emph{re-encoding} the token-identity information into a coordinate
system suited for deeper processing.

\section{The decoder-only architecture}

A GPT-style transformer consists of $L$ identical \textbf{decoder
layers} stacked sequentially (there is no separate encoder).
Each layer transforms the hidden state $H^{(l,t)} \to H^{(l+1,t)}$
through two sub-components applied in sequence: \emph{causal}
self-attention and a feed-forward network.

\subsection*{Self-attention}
\label{sec:self-attention}

Self-attention is the mechanism by which tokens communicate.  At each
layer, every token ``looks at'' all other tokens and computes a
weighted combination of their representations.  Here is the math.

Each hidden state $H^{(l,t)} \in \mathbb{R}^p$ is projected through
three learned matrices to produce a \textbf{query}, \textbf{key}, and
\textbf{value}:
\begin{equation}
  q_t = W_Q \, H^{(l,t)}, \qquad
  k_t = W_K \, H^{(l,t)}, \qquad
  v_t = W_V \, H^{(l,t)}
\end{equation}
where $W_Q, W_K \in \mathbb{R}^{d_k \times p}$ and
$W_V \in \mathbb{R}^{d_v \times p}$ are learned weight matrices, and
$d_k, d_v$ are the query/key and value dimensions
(typically $d_k = d_v = p / n_{\text{heads}}$).

Think of it this way: the query $q_t$ asks ``what am I looking for?'',
the key $k_s$ says ``here is what I contain,'' and the value $v_s$ says
``here is the information I provide if selected.''

The \textbf{attention weight} from token $t$ to token $s$ measures how
well the query matches the key:
\begin{equation}
  \alpha_{t,s} = \frac{\exp(q_t^\top k_s \,/\, \sqrt{d_k})}
                      {\sum_{r \le t} \exp(q_t^\top k_r \,/\, \sqrt{d_k})}
\end{equation}
The division by $\sqrt{d_k}$ prevents the dot products from growing too
large in high dimensions.  The $\exp$ and normalisation (a
\textbf{softmax}) turn the raw scores into probabilities that sum to~1.
The constraint $r \le t$ is the \textbf{causal mask}: token $t$ can
only attend to tokens at or before its own position (not to future
tokens).

The attention output for token $t$ is the weighted sum of values:
\begin{equation}
  \mathrm{Attn}(t) = \sum_{s \le t} \alpha_{t,s} \, v_s
\end{equation}

In practice, transformers use \textbf{multi-head attention}: the
projection is split into $n_{\text{heads}}$ independent ``heads,'' each
with its own $W_Q, W_K, W_V$ matrices of smaller dimension.  Each head
can attend to different aspects of the input (one head might track
syntactic relationships while another tracks semantic similarity).
The outputs of all heads are concatenated and projected back to
$\mathbb{R}^p$:
\begin{equation}
  \mathrm{MultiHead}(t) = W_O \,
    \begin{bmatrix} \mathrm{head}_1(t) \\ \vdots \\ \mathrm{head}_{n_h}(t) \end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}
where $W_O \in \mathbb{R}^{p \times p}$ is a learned output projection.

The result: each token's representation is updated based on all tokens
that precede it.  The same word is represented differently depending on
its context --- ``bank'' in ``river bank'' vs.\ ``bank account'' will
have different hidden states after attention.

\subsection*{Feed-forward network (MLP)}

After attention, each token is processed \emph{independently} by a
two-layer neural network:
\begin{equation}
  \mathrm{FFN}(x) = W_2 \;\sigma(W_1 \, x + b_1) + b_2
\end{equation}
where $W_1 \in \mathbb{R}^{d_{\mathrm{ff}} \times p}$ and
$W_2 \in \mathbb{R}^{p \times d_{\mathrm{ff}}}$ are learned weight
matrices, $b_1, b_2$ are biases, and $\sigma$ is a nonlinear
activation function (modern transformers typically use
SiLU or GELU).  The intermediate dimension
$d_{\mathrm{ff}}$ is usually $4p$ or larger ($d_{\mathrm{ff}} = 18944$
for Qwen2.5-7B).

The MLP is the model's ``thinking'' step for each token --- it combines
and transforms features without inter-token communication.

\subsection*{Residual connections and layer normalisation}

Both sub-components use \textbf{residual connections}: the output is
added to the input rather than replacing it:
\begin{equation}
  H^{(l+1,t)} = H^{(l,t)} + \mathrm{Attn}^{(l)}(t) + \mathrm{FFN}^{(l)}(t)
\end{equation}
This means the hidden state \emph{accumulates} changes layer by layer
rather than being overwritten.  The residual structure is what makes the
hidden-state grid continuous enough for GA to work: adjacent layers
differ by a small additive update.

\textbf{Layer normalisation} (applied before each sub-component)
rescales the hidden state to have zero mean and unit variance across
its dimensions, stabilising training.

The hidden state $H^{(l, t)} \in \mathbb{R}^p$ at layer $l$ and token
position $t$ is the model's internal representation --- the central
object of this book.

\section{The hidden-state grid}

The full set of hidden states forms a two-dimensional grid:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Layer axis}: $l = 0, 1, \ldots, L-1$, from input
  embedding to final representation.
\item \textbf{Token axis}: $t = 0, 1, \ldots, T-1$, from the first
  token to the last.
\end{itemize}
Each grid point holds a $p$-dimensional vector.  This $(L \times T)$
grid is the ``laboratory'' we study with GA.  Moving along the layer
axis corresponds to the model's processing depth; moving along the
token axis corresponds to the model's processing breadth.

\section{Autoregressive prediction}

The defining feature of a decoder-only model is \textbf{autoregressive
generation}: it predicts one token at a time, always conditioning on
everything that came before:
\begin{equation}
  P(x_t \mid x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_{t-1})
\end{equation}
This is why the causal mask exists in self-attention --- token $t$ must
not see tokens $t+1, t+2, \ldots$ because they have not been generated
yet.

\subsection*{The unembedding layer (decoding)}

After the final transformer layer, the hidden state must be converted
back into a prediction over words.  This is done by the
\textbf{unembedding layer} (also called the \textbf{language model
head}), a learned linear map
$W_{\mathrm{head}} \in \mathbb{R}^{V \times p}$:
\begin{equation}
  \mathrm{logits} = W_{\mathrm{head}} \, H^{(L-1, t)}
\end{equation}
where $V$ is the vocabulary size (e.g., $V = 151{,}936$ for
Qwen2.5-7B).  Each entry of the resulting $V$-dimensional vector is
the \textbf{logit} (unnormalised score) for one vocabulary token.
Applying a softmax converts logits to probabilities:
\begin{equation}
  P(x_{t+1} = w \mid x_{\le t})
    = \frac{\exp(\mathrm{logit}_w)}
           {\sum_{w'=1}^{V} \exp(\mathrm{logit}_{w'})}
\end{equation}
The highest-probability token is selected as the prediction.

Geometrically, the logit for word $w$ is the dot product of
$H^{(L-1,t)}$ with the $w$-th row of $W_{\mathrm{head}}$.  This means
the final hidden state must \emph{point in the right direction} for the
correct prediction to win.  This directional requirement is the
ultimate driver of the rotation activity we observe throughout the
network:

\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Early layers} perform large rotations (Chapter~6: up to
  $245^\circ$) to re-encode the raw embedding into an orientation from
  which deeper layers can work.
\item \textbf{Middle layers} perform context-dependent rotations --- the
  planes shift depending on what the model has read (Chapter~7).  This
  is where meaning is built: the hidden state is steered toward the
  correct region of representation space.
\item \textbf{Late layers} shift from rotation to stretching
  (Chapter~8): condition numbers exceed $10^3$ and effective ranks drop
  below~10.  The metric factor $P^{(l)}$ aggressively amplifies the
  target direction and suppresses competitors, funnelling the hidden
  state toward the correct vocabulary vector.
\end{itemize}

In the language of this book: rotation \emph{steers} (grade-2),
stretching \emph{selects} (grade-0).  Both are driven by the
dot-product geometry of the unembedding layer.

\subsection*{Autoregressive generation}

The \textbf{score} $s(x, u)$ of a token $u$ at the end of text $x$ is
the logit assigned to $u$ --- this is what the dependency gradient
(Chapter~\ref{chap:dependency}) differentiates through.

To generate a full sentence, the process repeats: the predicted token
is appended to the input, and the model runs again to predict the next
token, one step at a time.  At each step, the causal mask ensures the
model sees only the tokens generated so far.

\section{What gradients measure}

A \textbf{gradient} is the derivative of a scalar output with respect
to an input.  If the model's score for token ``Paris'' is
$s$, then $\partial s / \partial H^{(l,t)}$ is a vector pointing in
the direction of hidden-state change that most increases the score for
``Paris'' at layer~$l$, token~$t$.  Its magnitude tells you how
\emph{sensitive} the output is to changes at that point.

In this book, we use gradients to compute \textbf{dependency density}
(Chapter~\ref{chap:dependency}): $D_{l,t} = \|\partial s / \partial
H^{(l,t)}\|$.  This tells us which layers and tokens matter most for
the model's prediction --- without altering the model or its output.

\section{The models we study}

\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Qwen2.5-7B}: 28 layers, hidden dimension 3584, 7 billion
  parameters.  Our primary laboratory throughout the book.
\item \textbf{Qwen2.5-32B}: 64 layers, hidden dimension 5120, 32
  billion parameters.  Used for scaling comparisons.
\item \textbf{DeepSeek-R1-Distill-Qwen-7B}: same architecture as
  Qwen-7B, distilled from a larger reasoning model.  Used to study
  how training objective affects geometric structure.
\end{itemize}

All models are freely available on Hugging Face and can be loaded with
a single line of code (see the Preface).  A GPU with 16\,GB of memory
is sufficient for the 7B models.


% ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\chapter{GA from Scratch}
\label{app:ga-formal}
% ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

This appendix builds up Geometric Algebra from first principles.
If you have taken a linear algebra course and are comfortable with
vectors, dot products, and matrices, you have everything you need.
We introduce one idea at a time, with small examples you can verify
by hand.

\section{The problem GA solves}

Linear algebra gives you two operations on vectors: the \textbf{dot
product} $a \ip b$ (a scalar) and the \textbf{cross product}
$a \times b$ (a vector, but only in 3D).  The dot product tells you
about alignment; the cross product tells you about the plane the two
vectors span.

But the cross product is a 3D accident.  In $\mathbb{R}^{256}$ (the
kind of space we encounter inside transformers), there is no cross
product.  Yet we still want to talk about the plane that two vectors
define, the rotation that maps one vector to another, and how
rotations compose.

Geometric Algebra solves this by introducing a single product --- the
\textbf{geometric product} --- that captures both alignment and plane
information in any dimension.  Everything else follows.

\section{The geometric product: one rule}

Start with a vector space $V = \mathbb{R}^k$ equipped with the
standard inner product.  The \textbf{geometric product} is an
associative product on vectors defined by one rule:

\begin{gabox}
\textbf{The fundamental identity.}  For any vector $v$:
\begin{equation}
  v \, v = v \ip v = \|v\|^2
\end{equation}
The geometric product of a vector with itself is its squared length.
\end{gabox}

That's it.  This single rule, combined with associativity and
distributivity, generates the entire algebra.

Let's see what this implies.  Pick an orthonormal basis
$e_1, e_2, \ldots, e_k$ (so $e_i \ip e_j = \delta_{ij}$ (that is, $\delta_{ij} = 1$ when $i = j$ and $0$ otherwise)).
From the fundamental identity:
\begin{equation}
  e_i \, e_i = e_i \ip e_i = 1
\end{equation}

Now consider the product $e_1 \, e_2$.  We can derive its properties.
Compute $(e_1 + e_2)^2$ in two ways:

\textbf{By the fundamental identity:}
$(e_1 + e_2)^2 = \|e_1 + e_2\|^2 = 2$

\textbf{By expanding:}
$(e_1 + e_2)^2 = e_1 e_1 + e_1 e_2 + e_2 e_1 + e_2 e_2
= 1 + e_1 e_2 + e_2 e_1 + 1 = 2 + e_1 e_2 + e_2 e_1$

Comparing: $e_1 e_2 + e_2 e_1 = 0$.  This gives us:

\begin{gabox}
\textbf{Anti-commutativity of orthogonal vectors.}
\begin{equation}
  e_i \, e_j = -e_j \, e_i \qquad (i \neq j)
\end{equation}
Orthogonal vectors anti-commute under the geometric product.
\end{gabox}

For general vectors $a$ and $b$, the geometric product decomposes as:
\begin{equation}
  a \, b = \underbrace{a \ip b}_{\text{scalar (grade-0)}}
         + \underbrace{a \op b}_{\text{bivector (grade-2)}}
\end{equation}
The \textbf{inner product} $a \ip b$ is the familiar dot product.
The \textbf{outer product} $a \op b$ is something new: a
\emph{bivector}, which represents the oriented plane spanned by $a$
and $b$.

\paragraph{A small example.}
In $\mathbb{R}^3$, let $a = 3e_1 + e_2$ and $b = e_1 + 2e_2$.  Then:
\begin{align}
  a \ip b &= 3 \cdot 1 + 1 \cdot 2 = 5 \\
  a \op b &= (3 \cdot 2 - 1 \cdot 1)\, e_1 \op e_2
           = 5 \, e_1 \op e_2
\end{align}
So $a\,b = 5 + 5\,e_1 \op e_2$.
The scalar part says the vectors are partially aligned.  The bivector
part says they span an area of 5 in the $e_1 e_2$ plane.

\section{The Clifford algebra $\mathrm{Cl}(k, 0)$}

The algebra generated by all products of basis vectors $e_1, \ldots, e_k$
under the geometric product is the \textbf{Clifford algebra}
$\mathrm{Cl}(k, 0)$.  The ``$(k, 0)$'' means $k$ dimensions where every basis vector squares to $+1$ (as opposed to signatures used in relativity, which we never need). Here, `metric' just means the rule for computing squared lengths --- the standard dot product.

The distinct basis elements are:
\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}l l l l@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Grade} & \textbf{Basis elements} & \textbf{Count} & \textbf{Name} \\
\midrule
0 & $1$ & $\binom{k}{0} = 1$ & Scalars \\
1 & $e_1, e_2, \ldots, e_k$ & $\binom{k}{1} = k$ & Vectors \\
2 & $e_1 e_2, \; e_1 e_3, \; \ldots$ & $\binom{k}{2} = \frac{k(k-1)}{2}$ & Bivectors \\
3 & $e_1 e_2 e_3, \; \ldots$ & $\binom{k}{3}$ & Trivectors \\
$\vdots$ & $\vdots$ & $\vdots$ & $\vdots$ \\
$k$ & $e_1 e_2 \cdots e_k$ & $1$ & Pseudoscalar \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

The total dimension is $\sum_{r=0}^k \binom{k}{r} = 2^k$.  A general
element of $\mathrm{Cl}(k, 0)$ is a \textbf{multivector}: a sum of
components across different grades.

For $k = 3$: $\mathrm{Cl}(3,0)$ has dimension $2^3 = 8$, with
1~scalar, 3~vectors, 3~bivectors, and 1~pseudoscalar.

For $k = 256$ (the whitened dimension used in this book): $\mathrm{Cl}(256, 0)$
has dimension $2^{256}$, an astronomically large space.  But we only
ever use grades 0, 1, and 2 --- scalars, vectors, and bivectors --- which
is a subspace of dimension $1 + 256 + 32{,}640 = 32{,}897$.

\section{Blades and multivectors}

A \textbf{blade} is a grade-$r$ element that can be written as a single
outer product:
\begin{equation}
  B_r = v_1 \op v_2 \op \cdots \op v_r
\end{equation}

A blade represents an oriented $r$-dimensional subspace.  For grade-2
(bivectors), a blade is a single oriented plane.  A general bivector
may be a sum of blades --- this happens when a rotation involves
multiple planes simultaneously.

\paragraph{Example: simple vs compound bivectors.}
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item $B = 3 \, e_1 \op e_2$ is a \textbf{simple} bivector (a blade):
  it represents one plane.
\item $B = 2 \, e_1 \op e_2 + 5 \, e_3 \op e_4$ is a \textbf{compound}
  bivector: it involves two independent planes.  This cannot be written
  as a single $v_1 \op v_2$.
\end{itemize}

In 3D, every bivector is simple (there is only one independent plane).
In 4D and above, compound bivectors exist and are common.  In the
256-dimensional space of transformer hidden states, layer transition
bivectors are highly compound --- they involve dozens of active planes.
This is where the ``principal plane decomposition'' from Chapter~3
becomes essential.

\section{Rotors: the GA way to rotate}

A \textbf{rotor} is the GA object that represents a rotation. It is built from scalars and bivectors only (no plain vectors --- this is called `even-grade'). It must satisfy $R\widetilde{R} = 1$, where $\widetilde{R}$ reverses the order of all vector factors (defined precisely in the Reversion section below). Think of a rotor as the GA analog of a unit-length complex number $e^{i\theta}$.

The simplest rotor rotates by angle $\theta$ in the plane of unit
bivector $\hat{B}$:
\begin{equation}
  R = \exp\!\left(-\frac{\hat{B}\theta}{2}\right)
    = \cos\!\left(\frac{\theta}{2}\right)
    - \hat{B}\,\sin\!\left(\frac{\theta}{2}\right)
\end{equation}

Why the half angle?  Because a rotor acts on a vector $v$ via the
\textbf{sandwich product}:
\begin{equation}
  v' = R \, v \, \rev{R}
\end{equation}
The two factors of $R$ each contribute $\theta/2$, giving a total
rotation of $\theta$.

\paragraph{Example in $\mathbb{R}^3$.}
To rotate $e_1$ by $90^\circ$ in the $e_1 e_2$ plane:
\begin{align}
  R &= \cos(45^\circ) - e_1 e_2 \sin(45^\circ)
     = \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}(1 - e_1 e_2) \\
  R \, e_1 \, \rev{R}
    &= \frac{1}{2}(1 - e_1 e_2)\, e_1 \,(1 + e_1 e_2) \\
    &= \frac{1}{2}(e_1 - e_1 e_2 e_1)(1 + e_1 e_2) \\
    &= \frac{1}{2}(e_1 + e_2)(1 + e_1 e_2) \\
    &= e_2 \quad \checkmark
\end{align}
(We used $e_1 e_2 e_1 = -e_2 e_1 e_1 = -e_2$ and similar rules.)

\paragraph{Multi-plane rotors.}
In high dimensions, a rotor can involve multiple independent rotation
planes simultaneously.  A general rotor in $\mathbb{R}^k$ is:
\begin{equation}
  R = \exp\!\left(-\frac{1}{2}\sum_j \theta_j \hat{B}_j\right)
\end{equation}
where each $\hat{B}_j$ is a unit bivector in a distinct plane.
If the planes are orthogonal (commuting bivectors), this factorises
into a product of simple rotors:
$R = R_1 R_2 \cdots R_m$.

\section{The spin group}

The set of all rotors forms a group under the geometric product,
called the \textbf{spin group} $\mathrm{Spin}(k)$.

Key properties:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Closure}: the product of two rotors is a rotor.
\item \textbf{Identity}: $R = 1$ (the scalar 1 is a rotor with zero rotation).
\item \textbf{Inverse}: $R^{-1} = \rev{R}$ (just reverse the rotor).
\item \textbf{$\mathrm{SO}(k)$}: In matrix language, $\mathrm{SO}(k)$ is the group of $k \times k$
  orthogonal matrices with determinant $+1$ --- in other words, the group of all rotations in $k$ dimensions.
\item \textbf{Double cover}: both $R$ and $-R$ produce the same rotation (since $(-R)v(-\widetilde{R}) = Rv\widetilde{R}$). This means there are always two rotors for every rotation matrix --- a bookkeeping detail that never affects computations. So $\mathrm{Spin}(k)$ is a
  double cover of $\mathrm{SO}(k)$.
\end{itemize}

The spin group provides a
more compact and algebraically natural parameterisation: instead of
$k^2$ matrix entries subject to $k(k+1)/2$ orthogonality constraints,
a rotor is determined by a bivector (the logarithm), which has exactly
$k(k-1)/2$ free parameters --- the right number for a rotation in $k$
dimensions.

\section{Bivectors as rotation generators}
\textit{(the Lie algebra $\mathfrak{so}(k)$)}

Bivectors do double duty: they represent oriented planes \emph{and}
they are the building blocks of rotations: a bivector $B$ describes the direction and rate of a rotation, and exponentiating gives the full rotation $R = e^{-B/2}$. The bivector is to the rotation what velocity is to position.

Given two bivectors (two rotation directions), we can ask: does the order matter? The \textbf{commutator} answers this question. The set of all
bivectors, equipped with the \textbf{commutator bracket}
\begin{equation}
  [B_1, B_2] = B_1 B_2 - B_2 B_1
\end{equation}
forms an algebraic structure called a \textbf{Lie algebra} --- a vector space with a bracket operation that measures non-commutativity. This particular Lie algebra is the one that governs rotations in $k$ dimensions, known as $\mathfrak{so}(k)$.

The connection is explicit: a bivector
$B = \sum_{i < j} B_{ij} \, e_i \op e_j$
corresponds to the skew-symmetric matrix with entries $A_{ij} = B_{ij}$,
$A_{ji} = -B_{ij}$.  The commutator of bivectors maps directly to the
matrix commutator $[A_1, A_2] = A_1 A_2 - A_2 A_1$.

This Lie algebra has dimension $k(k-1)/2$.  For $k = 256$, that is
32,640 --- the number of independent bivector components, and
equivalently the number of independent rotation planes.

The commutator $[B_1, B_2]$ measures how much two rotations fail to
commute.  If $[B_1, B_2] = 0$, the rotations are independent (they can
be applied in either order).  If $[B_1, B_2] \neq 0$, the order matters
--- and the commutator bivector tells you \emph{which plane} the
non-commutativity lives in.

\section{Reversion}

The \textbf{reverse} $\rev{M}$ of a multivector reverses the order of
all vector factors in each term:
\begin{equation}
  \rev{(v_1 v_2 \cdots v_r)} = v_r \cdots v_2 v_1
\end{equation}

For a grade-$r$ element, this introduces a sign: $\rev{B_r} = (-1)^{r(r-1)/2} B_r$.
In practice:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item Grade 0 (scalar): $\rev{s} = s$
\item Grade 1 (vector): $\rev{v} = v$
\item Grade 2 (bivector): $\rev{B} = -B$
\item Grade 3 (trivector): $\rev{T} = -T$
\end{itemize}

Reversion is the GA analog of matrix transposition.  For a rotor
$R = \cos(\theta/2) - B\sin(\theta/2)$, the reverse is
$\rev{R} = \cos(\theta/2) + B\sin(\theta/2) = R^{-1}$, which is the
inverse rotation.

\section{Duality and the pseudoscalar}

The \textbf{pseudoscalar} is the highest-grade basis element:
\begin{equation}
  I_k = e_1 \, e_2 \, \cdots \, e_k
\end{equation}

It has a remarkable property: multiplying by $I_k$ maps a grade-$r$
element to a grade-$(k-r)$ element.  This is called \textbf{duality}.

\paragraph{In 3D.}
$I_3 = e_1 e_2 e_3$ has $I_3^2 = -1$.  The dual of a bivector is a
vector:
\begin{equation}
  (e_1 \op e_2)\, I_3^{-1} = e_3
\end{equation}
This is exactly the cross product: $a \times b = (a \op b)\, I_3^{-1}$.
The cross product is secretly a bivector in disguise, projected down
to a vector via duality.

\paragraph{In higher dimensions.}
There is no cross product in $\mathbb{R}^k$ for $k > 3$ (the dual of a
bivector is a $(k-2)$-vector, not a vector).  But the bivector itself
is perfectly well-defined.  This is the key advantage of GA: bivectors
work in any dimension, while the cross product is stuck in 3D.

\section{What we use in this book}

This book restricts attention to three grades:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Grade 0}: scalars --- inner products, stretching factors,
  eigenvalues.
\item \textbf{Grade 1}: vectors --- hidden states, basis vectors.
\item \textbf{Grade 2}: bivectors --- rotation planes, commutators,
  curvature, holonomy.
\end{itemize}

We never need trivectors or higher-grade elements, because the
operations we study (rotations, stretching, parallel transport) live entirely in the sub-algebra generated by grades~0 and~2 (scalars and bivectors). In the literature this is called the \textbf{even sub-algebra} $\mathrm{Cl}^+(k,0)$, but you do not need this name to follow the book.

The matrix representations used throughout the book are:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item Vectors: $\mathbb{R}^k$ arrays.
\item Bivectors: $k \times k$ skew-symmetric matrices
  ($A_{ij} = -A_{ji}$).
\item Rotors: $k \times k$ orthogonal matrices ($R R^\top = I$),
  with the bivector recovered via the matrix logarithm, Cayley map,
  or Rodrigues formula.
\end{itemize}

This is why the \texttt{layer\_time\_ga} library stores bivectors as
NumPy arrays rather than as $2^k$-dimensional multivector objects:
in the grade-$\{0, 1, 2\}$ sector, the matrix representation is both
compact and computationally efficient.


% ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
\chapter{The Matrix--Multivector Bridge}
\label{app:bridge}
% ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5}\small
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{@{}l l X@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Matrix operation} & \textbf{GA equivalent} & \textbf{What GA adds} \\
\midrule
Dot product $a^\top b$ & Inner product $a \ip b$ &
  Same computation \\
$ab^\top - ba^\top$ (skew) & Outer product $a \op b$ &
  Named as a bivector (oriented plane) \\
Skew-symmetric $A$ & Bivector $B$ &
  Principal plane decomposition \\
Orthogonal matrix $U$ (rotation, det $= +1$) & Rotor $R = e^{-B/2}$ &
  Explicit plane + angle \\
Polar decomposition $T = UP$ & Versor decomposition $T = RP$ &
  Rotation (plane information) cleanly separated from stretching (magnitude information) \\
$\|U - I\|_F$ & Rotor angle $\theta$ &
  Plus: which plane \\
$A_i A_j - A_j A_i$ & $[B_i, B_j]$ &
  Plus: decompose into planes \\
$\|\Omega - I\|_F$ (curvature) & Holonomy rotor &
  Plus: curvature direction \\
Eigenvalues of $P$ & Grade-0 content &
  Natural grade hierarchy \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\end{center}

\chapter{Code Reference}
\label{app:code}
% ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

\section{Student API: \texttt{ltg\_ga}}

\begin{lstlisting}
import ltg_ga

model = ltg_ga.load_model("Qwen/Qwen2.5-7B")
result = ltg_ga.analyse("prompt", model=model)
result.summary()
result.plot_ga_summary(save_path="summary.png")

# Compare multiple prompts
ltg_ga.compare([r1, r2, r3], save_path="compare.png")

# Capacity analysis
cap = ltg_ga.capacity("prompt", model=model)
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Core algebra: \texttt{layer\_time\_ga.algebra}}

\begin{lstlisting}
from layer_time_ga.algebra import (
    Bivector, Rotor,
    bivector_from_skew, rotor_from_orthogonal,
    rotor_compose, rotor_inverse,
    commutator_bivector, grade_decomposition,
    geometric_product_vectors,
)
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Decomposition: \texttt{layer\_time\_ga.decomposition}}

\begin{lstlisting}
from layer_time_ga.decomposition import (
    VersorDecomposition, LayerRotorField,
    versor_decompose, extract_rotor_field,
    extract_bivector_field, extract_metric_field,
)
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Curvature: \texttt{layer\_time\_ga.curvature}}

\begin{lstlisting}
from layer_time_ga.curvature import (
    holonomy_rotor, holonomy_field, holonomy_scalar_map,
    commutator_field, commutator_bivectors,
    commutator_plane_decomposition,
)
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Capacity: \texttt{layer\_time\_ga.capacity}}

\begin{lstlisting}
from layer_time_ga.capacity import (
    ga_capacity_profile, GACapacityProfile,
)
\end{lstlisting}

\section{BCA: \texttt{layer\_time\_ga.bca}}

Bivector Component Analysis decomposes the lagged second-moment matrix
of a frontier trajectory into symmetric (PCA) and antisymmetric
(bivector / directed flow) components
(Section~\ref{sec:pred-bca}).

\begin{lstlisting}[caption={BCA module API}]
from layer_time_ga.bca import (
    bca_decompose,       # single lag decomposition -> BCAResult
    bca_lag_sweep,       # sweep across lags -> BCALagSweep
    bca_rolling,         # rolling-window BCA -> RollingBCAResult
    bca_phase_portrait,  # phase portrait on bivector planes
    frontier_bca,        # convenience: GenerationResult -> BCAResult
)

# Quick usage with a GenerationResult:
r = frontier_bca(gen.frontier_whitened, lag=1)
print(f"Vorticity: {r.vorticity:.2f}")
print(f"Coherence: {r.coherence:.4f}")
print(f"Asymmetry: {r.asymmetry_ratio:.4f}")

# Lag sweep to detect characteristic time scales:
sweep = bca_lag_sweep(F_avg, lags=range(1, 16))

# Rolling window for temporal evolution:
roll = bca_rolling(F_avg, window=15, lag=1)

# Phase portrait on principal bivector planes:
portrait = bca_phase_portrait(
    {'Retrieval': F_ret, 'Reasoning': F_reas},
    lag=1, n_planes=2,
)
\end{lstlisting}

\backmatter

\chapter*{Glossary}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Glossary}

\begin{center}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.5}\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}L{4cm} L{7.5cm}@{}}
\toprule
\rowcolor{Navy}
\textcolor{white}{\textbf{Term}} &
\textcolor{white}{\textbf{Definition}} \\
\midrule
BCA (Bivector Component Analysis) &
  Decomposition of the lagged second-moment matrix into symmetric
  (PCA) and antisymmetric (bivector) components; the antisymmetric
  part encodes directed temporal flow. \\
BCcos (Binet--Cauchy cosine) &
  A signed measure of oriented-area overlap between two bivector planes;
  ranges from $-1$ (anti-aligned) through $0$ (orthogonal) to $+1$ (co-planar). \\
Bivector &
  A grade-2 element representing an oriented plane; stored as a
  skew-symmetric matrix. \\
Blade &
  A bivector (or higher-grade element) that can be written as a single
  outer product $a \op b$; represents one plane. \\
Capacity ($C_{\mathrm{acc}}$) &
  Accumulated non-commutativity $\sum \|[B^{(l)}, B^{(l+1)}]\|_F$;
  measures total geometric computation across layers. \\
Cayley bivector &
  The skew-symmetric matrix $A = (I - U)(I + U)^{-1}$ extracted from
  a rotation~$U$; its principal planes are the rotation's eigenplanes. \\
Clifford algebra &
  The algebra $\mathrm{Cl}(k, 0)$ generated by vectors under
  $v^2 = \|v\|^2$ (i.e., squaring a vector gives its squared length).  Contains all grades from 0 to $k$. \\
Commutator &
  $[B_1, B_2] = B_1 B_2 - B_2 B_1$; a bivector measuring
  non-commutativity. \\
Curvature bivector &
  $\log(R_{\text{loop}})$: the plane in which space is curved at a
  grid point; obtained from the holonomy rotor. \\
Dependency ($D_l$) &
  $\|\partial s / \partial H^{(l)}\|_F$: gradient-based measure of how
  much the output depends on layer~$l$'s hidden state. \\
Directional flow ratio ($\rho^{(l)}$) &
  $\|A^{(l)}\|_F / (\|S^{(l)}\|_F + \varepsilon)$: ratio of
  grade-2 (rotation) to grade-0 (stretch) content. \\
Frontier &
  The sequence of layer-time grid points visited during autoregressive
  decoding; each decode step adds a new column to the grid. \\
Geometric product &
  $ab = a \ip b + a \op b$; the fundamental operation of GA. \\
Grade &
  The ``type'' of an algebraic element: 0 = scalar, 1 = vector,
  2 = bivector. \\
Holonomy &
  The rotor obtained by composing rotors around one cell of the layer-token grid; measures
  curvature. \\
Jacobian &
  The matrix of all first-order partial derivatives of a vector
  function; entry $(i,j)$ records how the $i$-th output changes with
  the $j$-th input.  For a linear layer $H^{(l+1)} \approx T\,H^{(l)}$,
  the Jacobian is $T$ itself. \\
Metric factor &
  The symmetric positive-definite matrix $P$ from $T = RP$; encodes
  grade-0 stretching. \\
Nonseparability index ($\mathcal{D}$) &
  $\sum \|R_{\text{loop}} - I\|_F$: total holonomy deficit across the
  layer-token grid; measures geometric entanglement. \\
Orthonormal frame &
  Basis vectors with $e_i \ip e_j = 1$ when $i=j$ and $0$ otherwise; required for valid GA
  operations. \\
Principal plane &
  A simple-bivector component of a compound bivector; extracted via
  eigendecomposition. \\
Reversion &
  The GA analog of transpose: $\rev{R} = R^{-1}$ for rotors. \\
Rotor &
  An even-grade element $R = \exp(-B\theta/2)$ representing a rotation
  in plane $B$ by angle $\theta$. \\
Sandwich product &
  $v' = R v \rev{R}$; how a rotor acts on a vector. \\
$\tau$ (Cayley magnitude) &
  $\|A\|_F / \sqrt{2} = \tan(\theta/2)$: a scalar summary of rotation
  strength from the Cayley bivector. \\
Versor decomposition &
  $T = RP$: separating rotation (grade-2) from stretching (grade-0). \\
Vorticity ($\Omega$) &
  Sum of BCA rotation strengths $\sum_j \omega_j$; measures total
  directed temporal flow in a frontier trajectory. \\
Coherence ($\Psi$) &
  $\omega_1 / \Omega$: fraction of directed flow in the dominant
  bivector plane; high = concentrated, low = dispersed. \\
Whitening &
  PCA-based dimensionality reduction (PCA projection followed by unit-variance rescaling) that constructs an orthonormal frame. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}
